Builders Unscripted: Ep. 1 - Peter Steinberger, Creator of OpenClaw

OpenAI(1.9M) ·31:29 ·134.4K views · 2026-02-24
Peter Steinberger (Creator, OpenClaw) Romain Huet (Head of Developer Experience, OpenAI)
Peter Steinberger, creator of OpenClaw, discusses how "agentic engineering" has fundamentally shifted the developer's role from writing syntax to managing intent, enabling a level of productivity where a single builder can maintain over 120 projects and 90,000 annual contributions.
TL;DR Peter Steinberger, creator of OpenClaw, discusses how "agentic engineering" has fundamentally shifted the developer's role from writing syntax to managing intent, enabling a level of productivity where a single builder can maintain over 120 projects and 90,000 annual...

TL;DR

Peter Steinberger, creator of OpenClaw, discusses how "agentic engineering" has fundamentally shifted the developer's role from writing syntax to managing intent, enabling a level of productivity where a single builder can maintain over 120 projects and 90,000 annual contributions.

The Genesis of OpenClaw and Autonomous Problem-Solving

  • Emergence of a Phenomenon: OpenClaw transitioned from a personal "playground" to a global project in weeks, resulting in "ClawCon" events with thousands of attendees in San Francisco and hundreds in Vienna.
  • The "Aha" Moment in Autonomy: Steinberger realized the power of agents when his bot autonomously handled a voice message it wasn't programmed for.
      • Spontaneous Problem Solving: The agent identified an audio file without an extension, used `ffmpeg` to convert it, found an OpenAI API key in the environment, and used `curl` to transcribe the audio via Whisper—all without specific instructions.
  • The Personal Assistant Vision: OpenClaw is designed as a personal, highly privileged assistant that can access a user’s computer, WhatsApp, and deployment tools like Vercel to "yield things into existence."

The "Codex" Workflow: Scaling Human Output

  • Massive Productivity Gains: Steinberger recorded 90,000 GitHub contributions across 120+ projects in one year, a feat he attributes to mastering agentic harnesses.
  • Conversation as Engineering: Steinberger views programming now as a conversation rather than manual typing.
      • The "Any Questions?" Prompt: He emphasizes asking the model "Do you have any questions?" to prevent it from making incorrect default assumptions based on stale training data.
  • The "Agentic Trap": He warns against over-optimizing setups (the "agentic trap") and instead advocates for a "vibe coding" approach—focusing on architecture and gut feelings for how long a prompt will take to execute.
  • Accepting "Boring" Code: Steinberger argues that most code is simply data transformation. He accepts "slop" or code that isn't exactly how he would write it, provided it fulfills the architectural intent and passes performance requirements.

Redefining Open Source: From Pull Requests to "Prompt Requests"

  • Intent over Syntax: With 2,000+ open PRs, Steinberger no longer reviews every line of code. Instead, he uses agents to summarize the *intent* of a contributor's PR.
  • The AI Maintainer Workflow: He uses voice commands to discuss a PR's architectural fit with an agent. If the intent is sound, he uses a "land PR" command to have the agent rewrite the solution to fit the broader system.
  • Self-Modifying Software: OpenClaw's default installation is a `git clone` where the agent sits inside its own source code, allowing it to modify itself based on user prompts.

Security Challenges and the "Hacker’s Paradise"

  • Unsolved Prompt Injection: Steinberger acknowledges that while prompt injection is a concern, modern models are increasingly resilient. He uses a "soldier.md" file to define core values and operational boundaries for his agents.
  • The Public Internet Risk: A significant friction point exists between Steinberger’s vision of a "hacker’s paradise" (accessible via private networks) and security researchers who flag the lack of enterprise-grade login limits as a vulnerability when users mistakenly expose the tool to the public internet.
  • Sandboxing Evolution: To mitigate risks, he transitioned OpenClaw from running directly on a Mac Studio ("the castle") to localized containers.

Strategic Advice for Builders

  • The Playful Mindset: Steinberger advises developers to approach AI tools "playfully" to avoid frustration. He views agentic engineering as a skill like playing the guitar—it requires time to develop a "gut feeling" for model behavior.
  • High Agency as the Core Value: He echoes the sentiment that AI will not replace developers; rather, developers who use AI effectively will replace those who do not.
  • Lowering the Barrier to Entry: The current toolchain allows builders to create solutions that solve 100% of their specific problems, even if an existing open-source tool already solves 70%.
"Software is still hard, but you're so much faster now... I had this realization that now I can build anything." — Peter Steinberger
"Most code is boring. Like most code just transforms one shape of data into another shape of data... In the end, you should optimize a codebase so agents can do their best work." — Peter Steinberger
"You're not going to be replaced by AI, you're going to be replaced by someone who uses AI better than you." — Peter Steinberger (quoting Jensen Huang)

"Do you have any questions?" is like a very important question. People don't realize that the model usually starts with a blank slate, because they don't learn like us. Like every new session is like "I know nothing about this codebase and I'll just search and find the little things that you asked me to and then try to solve it." But they don't see usually the whole picture. If it becomes a performance problem you just focus on that, you make it better. And your point that you just made on like you kind of like the value of code and how you approach code is also changing a lot how you approach open source, right? Because like I was looking at like OpenClaw, you now have like 2,000 PRs open right now. You know, before we had like AI, you would have to read all of these PRs because there was value in the code being created. But sometimes now you refer to that as like not a pull request but a prompt request almost, because it's like more the idea or the intention behind the PR that's more important than the code itself. PRs actually sometimes take me longer than if I would approach it myself because my trust in the model not being malicious is much higher than an external contributor that I never heard of before and where we had no discussion before. So I have to scrutinize that a little bit more. But then if I see a PR, like I start to review and my first question to the model is "Do you understand the intent of the PR?" Because I don't really care about the code, I care about what is that person actually trying to solve? You know, it's more like an issue with like a "Here's my attempt of a solution." But first of all, many many people still don't really know how to yield their agent, and then often it's just a very localized solution because they they don't have the full system in their head. The hard part is how does this little new feature fit into my whole my my bigger system, or how does this little fix, "Okay, this little fix fixes a tiny thing but is it even the right fix?" Probably often this is like a more systemic or an architecture issue. So the model is actually really good if you you just have a conversation, and then when I say "Okay, now build this," then it will start to build this. So I ask the model "What's the what's the intent? Is that the most optimal solution?" Sometimes it says yes, most often it would say no. Then I would start to explore what would be the what would be the best fix. Is this like an architectural problem? Is this something that is, let's say it's it's a problem in message handling. Does this only affect WhatsApp or maybe also I don't know Signal? So should we should we solve this in a more general way? Is this a new feature? Do we even want the new feature? So sometimes those discussions go on for like 10-15 minutes. I use like voice because it's literally like you you talk to a very smart coworker. Easier to give tokens over voice than like typing. Yeah, when when I'm happy then I have like one slash command, like "land PR," that explains the whole procedure to like actually create a branch, do all the changes, get the PR merged. I I wanted to create a community so I I try to still credit the person that created it even though the whole thing takes me longer than if I would just write it all myself. But I I appreciate that people want to be part of this. What's your vision for OpenClaw now going forward with all of these contributors that are kind of like surrounding the project and and do you see yourself as kind of a trailblazer for this idea of like a what a personal AI agent should look like so that, you know, a billion people can one day use something like that? Yeah, I want to find the balance between "this is something that my mom can install" but also "this is something that's fun and hackable," which is difficult. You know most open-source projects you like download a package, but for the longest time my default install was "git clone, build, run." And then you literally have the source code on disk and the agent sits in the source code and is aware of the source code, and if you don't like anything you literally prompt the agent and it would change itself. Like actual self-modifying software. So there are a lot of people that never sent me a PR sent me a PR. That's that's also why it's more like a prompt request because they just like the understanding of how to build software that that lasts. And at the same time the whole world is is or the whole security industry is like putting their eyes on it, which has been interesting and also a little bit frustrating because it it misses some nuance, right? For example the the web server that I have, that's meant for you. Initially I built it for debugging and then I made it pretty, but it's only meant to be accessible in your network, in your trusted network. But because it should also be like a hacker's a hacker's paradise, there is an option how you can like change that because like some people have weird setups, maybe they use like ngrok, maybe they use reverse proxies, so there is a reason why I didn't want to like limit that. But now you have like people that put it on the open internet, even though I'm screaming in the security document "Please don't do that, that's not that's not what it's intended for." And then I have the security people that point out that "Oh yeah, it doesn't have login limitations," you know, like "it doesn't do all these things that you need when it's on the public internet." I'm like "Yeah, I didn't build it for that with that intention." But because it is configurable it totally counts as a as a CVSS 10. So yeah, I I struggled a little bit with that. But now I I brought on actually a security expert that's the main focus. I I realize I cannot stop people from using it in ways it was not intended, so my my big focus now is to like support all these use cases and help people not shoot themselves in the foot. Right. Well that's the beauty of open source, people can embrace it and like come up with ideas that you didn't even think about. Yeah, that's the beauty and the madness of it. Zooming out maybe for a second beyond OpenClaw, like I've talked to a bunch of developers this week. We knew you were coming to the Codex Hackathon and they told me like "How many, how come does like Peter come up with all these great ideas? Like how does Peter come up with so much creativity?" I don't know if you have any answer to that or if it's more like your own curiosity that you're following. It's more like this realization that stuff's easy now. So even if I find an open-source thing that maybe solves my problem to 70%, I just build it myself, which would have been absolutely impossible a year ago even, and now it's just like I just prompt it then it's on a second screen and and Codex works. We're both from Europe. When I travel outside of SF and I go back to Europe, I'm sure you feel the same way, like many developers and engineers have not embraced Codex and agentic tools just yet. What would be your advice for them like as they get started and like should rethink the way they work and their workflow? My first advice always is like approach it in a playful way. Build something that you always wanted to build. I'm if you're at least a little bit of a builder there has to be something on the back of your mind that you want to build. Like just just play. You just have to approach this this thing playfully. Because like I think the Nvidia Nvidia CEO said that in the near term you're not going to be replaced by AI, you're going to be replaced by someone who uses AI better than you. But if your identity is "I want to I want to create things, I want to solve problems," if you're high agency if you're smart, you will be in more demand than ever. Yeah, it's such a great time for builders who embrace these tools, shape their curiosity and really can bring any idea to life the exact same way you've done with all these great projects and OpenClaw. How This Founder Went From Living In AOL's Offices To Running A Profitable AI Startup

Forbes(2.1M) ·28:22 ·800 views · 2026-02-24
Eric Simons (CEO, StackBlitz) Katharine Schwab (Journalist, Forbes)
StackBlitz transitioned from a struggling $500k ARR developer tool to a $20M ARR AI powerhouse in just two months by pivoting to "Bolt.new," a prompt-to-app platform now used by 75% of the Fortune 500.
TL;DR StackBlitz transitioned from a struggling $500k ARR developer tool to a $20M ARR AI powerhouse in just two months by pivoting to "Bolt.new," a prompt-to-app platform now used by 75% of the Fortune 500. CEO Eric Simons has since paused human hiring to prioritize "agentic...

TL;DR

StackBlitz transitioned from a struggling $500k ARR developer tool to a $20M ARR AI powerhouse in just two months by pivoting to "Bolt.new," a prompt-to-app platform now used by 75% of the Fortune 500. CEO Eric Simons has since paused human hiring to prioritize "agentic teams," aiming for a landmark $10M in gross margin per employee.

The Bolt.new Pivot: From $500k to $20M ARR

  • The Ultimatum: After seven years of building browser-based IDE technology (WebContainers) with minimal commercial traction, StackBlitz faced a board-level mandate: find a winning product by the end of 2024 or spin down the company.
  • Explosive Growth: Bolt.new, a "vibe coding" tool that builds full-stack web applications from English prompts, was the company's final experiment. It generated $20 million in ARR within its first 60 days.
  • The "Vibe Coding" Shift: Unlike traditional low-code tools, Bolt creates production-ready code.
      • Enterprise Adoption: 75% of Fortune 500 companies now use the tool to accelerate product development, moving directly from prompt to shippable product rather than stalling in Figma design phases.
      • The "Prototype is the Product": Simons emphasizes that the goal is to eliminate throwaway code, allowing PMs to generate functional prototypes that engineers can immediately move to production.

Strategy: Scaling via AI Agents, Not Headcount

  • Hiring Freeze: StackBlitz has effectively paused human hiring to prioritize the "hiring" and integration of AI agents across engineering, product, design, and marketing.
  • The 1:50 Ratio: Simons believes the future of work is one human managing a digital team of 50 agents. He argues one person with an agentic team is faster and more nimble than 50 people in a physical office.
  • Financial Efficiency Target: The company is targeting an unprecedented $10 million in gross margin per employee over the next 12 to 18 months.
  • Current Financials:
      • Profitability: The company is currently profitable.
      • Margins: Maintaining 70% gross margins, which Simons notes is significantly higher than many other AI-native startups.
      • Capital: StackBlitz has not yet spent the majority of its last funding round, choosing to grow via margin rather than venture burn.

Enterprise Differentiation and Workflow

  • Beyond "Toy" Apps: While many AI coding tools build simple "dog pages," StackBlitz focuses on the "durability of revenue" by solving enterprise-specific problems.
  • System Integration: To be useful for a company like Visa, an AI agent must have access to existing design systems, component libraries, and documentation to ensure the output matches brand standards and technical requirements.
  • Abstraction Layer: Simons compares Bolt to Netlify or Vercel. While it uses frontier models like Claude Code as a "workhorse," Bolt provides the "turnkey" experience that abstracts the technical complexity (e.g., Kubernetes, server setup) for the user.

Macro Economic Outlook and Education

  • The "Fake Work" Eradication: Simons views AI agents as a way to bypass "arcane processes" and "fake work," such as lengthy approval memos and design hand-offs, enabling visionaries within large companies to ship faster.
  • Educational Shift: Having skipped college himself to move to Silicon Valley (famously squatting in AOL’s offices for four months to survive), Simons argues that AI serves as the "world's best tutor."
  • Ubiquitous Access: He advocates for keeping these tools free/accessible to allow individuals to provide value independently, potentially bypassing traditional corporate or educational paths that aren't keeping pace with technology.
"We went from zero to 20 million of ARR in the first two months of [Bolt] being online, which is insane. We ended up not shutting down the company." — Eric Simons
"We're effectively pausing hiring as a company because right now we are prioritizing hiring of agents—of agentic systems... One person with a team of 50 agents is going to be faster than 50 people that's RTO in an office." — Eric Simons
"Our target over the next 12 to 18 months is to have the gross margin number be in the range of $10 million per person. It sounds kind of crazy to say out loud... but it seems inevitable." — Eric Simons
Hi, I am Katharine Schwab. I'm an Assistant Managing Editor here at Forbes covering technology, and I am stoked to be here today with Eric Simons. He is the CEO and co-founder of StackBlitz, which is most known for its AI code generator, Bolt. You can use it to type in a prompt and it will create any app or website that you want. Eric, thanks for joining me. Yeah, thank you for having me. I'm stoked to be here. Let's start by hearing a little bit more about what Bolt is and how people use it. Yeah, totally. Yeah, so Bolt is very simply—it is a website that allows you to type in a prompt in just, you know, normal English and it'll actually build full-stack web applications entirely in your browser. This category is kind of like called "text-to-app" or "vibe coding," and we were really the first vibe coding tool to hit the market back in October of 2024. Today, Bolt has exploded in usage. We've got, I think, you know, over 10 million people that have signed up for Bolt at this point. We're used by 75% of the Fortune 500, and so today a lot of our focus is really on helping companies accelerate their product development. Instead of dragging things around in Figma, they actually will just come to Bolt. Whenever they want to prototype a new feature in Bolt, they can just type in, "Hey, you know, I want to add this, you know, widget to our page," and it'll make it. It's actually shippable to production. So our kind of slogan is like, "The prototype is the product." So that's kind of the nature of what companies use this for—accelerating their product development cycles. Okay, great. So to rewind a little bit, I know you only launched Bolt in 2024, but StackBlitz dates much earlier. Talk to me a little bit about the journey of the company before you kind of hit on this really hot product. Yeah, yeah. So I started this company with actually one of my childhood best friends. He and I grew up down the street from each other in Chicago. I think we were like 13 when we learned how to code and build websites, and he and I have been building websites together ever since for the past a little over two decades now. And back in 2017, we started building StackBlitz, which was like the name of the company and the original product. The vision was really to allow people to build full-stack web applications on the web in the same way that Figma and Canva had brought design to the web. It was not like in the sense of like Wix, where it's like a drag-and-drop sort of thing or whatever and just simple websites, but like really building like real applications to the degree—like the same technologies that like Facebook and Google use, right, to build out their apps. And this technology didn't exist before we started working on this. And what we ended up—we ended up working on that product, the technology called WebContainers, that like basically like—some like kind of do a little nerd speak here—it boots an operating system in your browser with WebAssembly. It took us years to build that and people thought we were nuts; they did not think it would work. It ended up working, so it was like a three or four-year kind of deep technology bet. And then, the product was a web-based IDE for developers. And the thing with developers is that they are very opinionated. They thought what we built was very cool and they would use it like lightly for random prototyping, that sort of thing, but getting them out of their local dev environments was a huge challenge. And so, us—it was kind of a mirage of a market, cloud IDEs in the late 2010s and early 2020s. We were one of these products where there was a lot of hype, but the demand did not actually pan out to be a real business opportunity. And so going into 2024, it was kind of clear like we had to—we had to, you know, create a product that would actually change the trajectory of our business from a financial standpoint. Because I think at that point, we were doing, I don't know, $500,000 of ARR after seven years or something. So with our board, we decided well, if we can't really change the trajectory, we're going to have to spin down at the end of the year. So we tried out a whole bunch of different experiments in 2024. Bolt was the last one that we tried, and it worked better than I could have ever imagined. We went from like zero to 20 million of ARR in the first two months of it being online, which is insane. And so we ended up not shutting down the company, which was great, and now we're taking it to new heights. So that's kind of the condensed story of this thing. Yeah. And you also started a couple of companies before StackBlitz as well and I guess famously squatted in AOL's office for multiple months. Tell me a little bit more about your journey as an entrepreneur kind of before StackBlitz. I was wondering if that was going to come up, hoping that wasn't going to—I'm just kidding. But yeah, yeah. I mean, so when my co-founder and I first came out here, you know, we were—we had made the choice to not go to college because it was going to cost a ton of money. It was going to be like $30,000 a month to go to in-state at the University of Illinois or whatever. So we came out here. We're like already making money doing this, you know, as high schoolers. Let's just move out to Silicon Valley and figure out how to stay alive and ship software. And so we came out here basically broke and kind of long story short is, you know, we ended up going through a small accelerator at the time called Imagine K12—then it became part of Y Combinator a few years later. But they had access to AOL's headquarters because they were renting office space from them, and so we got badges to go into AOL's headquarters anytime, day or night. And in the process of us just going and visiting their office, I noticed that they had couches at AOL. They had a gym which had showers and even a laundry machine. They often had food in the office. Yeah, so you know, there's all these dots that connected. And so the 18 or 19-year-old version of me was like, "Huh, like we're having trouble making rent. What do we do?" And so I ended up living out of AOL's headquarters for, I think it was like four months or something like that. And whenever people usually ask like how—how did they not know, and the answer is it's like kind of classic big company stuff; like people don't, you know, aren't talking to each other a ton. So like, there were two shifts of security that I saw. It was like the daytime crew and the nighttime crew. So the daytime crew just saw me working and thought, "Like, this guy looks like a hard worker," and the nighttime crew would see me working and they'd be like, "Wow, this guy is a super hard worker. Man, he's even sleeping on the couch. Like, AOL should promote this guy," you know. And I don't know how they connected the dots. Eventually, they did, and then they—one morning at 4 in the morning, they threw me out and I slept on a Starbucks table outside because they weren't open yet. Wow, what a hustle. That was a long time ago, I should mention. That was like 15, 16 years ago. I've got a house I sleep in, I've got a kid, I've got a daughter, I have responsibilities, right, you know, and I'm a responsible adult now so—but yeah. Crazy, crazy, what a great story. I'm glad to hear that you're no longer in that place where you're crashing on a couch in an office. Yeah, I mean the growth of the company has been just really remarkable, but there's also a lot of vibe coding apps out there now and a lot of competition in this space. So who do you see as your biggest competitors and why do you feel like Bolt is unique? Yeah, I think you know, I mean when we launched Bolt, like, we were the first thing that came out and so the growth was just extraordinary. And you know, for the first month I would wake up every day just kind of expecting it to stop because that's like most things you launch like products. Because my co-founder and I have launched dozens of things, you know, throughout our career. And usually it's like you get a blip on the first day, it's like, "Yay!" and then—and then it all comes back down. Then you spend a long time grinding it out and then compounding, you know, improving the product and stuff over time. This one, it just kept growing. And it was kind of like a murder mystery because it was like, "What is going on? Who are these people using it? Why are they using it?" and you know, because they just—they came from all walks of life. But when it kind of clicked, though, was just, you know, it was clear that there's just this tectonic shift that was happening and we were the first product, you know, on that tectonic plate to hit. And it just became clear to me—this was going to—vibe coding was going to be a thing. I mean, this is going to be everywhere. There was going to be new startups competing; there's going to be incumbents shipping this stuff in their products. And you know, a year and a couple months later, that's exactly what has happened. And so the—you know, when we realized that, really we went into experimentation mode as far as, you know, who are the—there's tons of different types of customers using this—who are really the right ones to focus on? And that tends to be—when you—when you ask that question, you're really asking the question, "You know, what kind of business do we want to build? Like, what sorts of customers do we think are the right ones to really build products for?" And to us, what that really meant was durability of—of the revenue that was coming in. Like, who of these people who are actually going to use this every day in their workflows versus something they're going to use, you know, once a quarter or once a year, right? And so in the cohorts that we saw, it was really the businesses and enterprises using this to transform how they build products. Versus where a lot of the top of funnel for us and every other vibe coding tool are consumers or prosumers, which is great—it's like an important part of a product-led growth motion—but it's not actually the number one thing we're trying to like grow, right? Or the number one thing we're trying to build products or features in our products to go and serve. So for us, when we look at our competitors, we really look at what's—in who are the companies that are doing a great job of serving businesses in this new vibe coding AI-native frontier. And I don't—you know, I think God, everyone's competing with each other I suppose. I think the frontier labs—we keep an eye on because, you know, they—they—they're spending a lot of time going and selling to businesses. But when we look at the startups, there's actually not a lot of companies that are doing a super great job selling to existing businesses and enterprises today for this sort of functionality. I think Figma's done a good job with their product where they've added some AI capabilities and that sort of thing. But at this point for—kind of for what we see—there's—it's really more of a race against ourselves in a sense to go and build out the—this kind of deep, you know, capability suite in our products that'll actually allow businesses of every size and shape and type to—to adopt this sort of technology. Because I think that's really where a lot of the gap is today. If you're an average consumer wanting to play around with stuff, there's tons of options, it's super easy because you don't have to think about security, you don't have to think about tying it into some existing system or whatever. Not the case for businesses, and you know, there—there just hasn't been a ton of focus on that end from what we've seen. Interesting. I'd love to dig a little bit more on what those business use cases are. Could you give me a couple of different examples of how you've seen those enterprise customers start using this in their kind of daily workflows? Like you said, if that—if that daily workflow is really the goal for you all. Yeah, totally. I mean, yeah. So Bolt is really the tool that the engineering, product, and design teams will reach for when they're going to build out a really—a real prototype of how some functionality should lay into their product or on their public web pages or what have you. And the kind of key thing—and you again, you can kind of see this with like—pick any vibe coding tool, you'll get a sense of what—what I'm saying and kind of what it means in a general sense—where you can like go and you can say, "Make me a web page about my dog," and it'll like do that and you're like, "Wow," right? But what's different for an enterprise company—if you're like Visa, for example, when you—if you go and say, "Make me a landing page, you know, that's going to tell our customers about why our credit card is the best," you certainly don't want that web page coming back looking like it's from MasterCard, right? That would be very bad. But then that implies a number of things. That implies that the agent that's actually going and doing that work has access to all of the existing components that your company uses to ship into production, all of the designs, all of the, you know, documentation guides, whatever. So you need to have access to, you know, the repositories where that stuff exists, you need to have access to the documentation, you need to have an agentic system that knows how to actually piece those together in basically milliseconds from when the user hits—hits enter. And so that's really kind of the magic is like for any organization you can drop Bolt in, connect it to the various sources for your design system components, documentation, your services, whatever, and it can just get up and running and be producing prototypes that are not just like throwaway, you know, code that you can't use. These are—it's the same code that your engineers are pushing to production. And so that's huge because you can have someone—so a tangible workflow be: I am a PM at let's say Visa, as a random example. I think that we need to go and add a landing page, you know, for some new feature we're adding to our credit card. I go into Bolt, I say, "Here's kind of the spec of what I want this thing to say and look like and do." Hit enter. Maybe the first thing comes back—looks great. I might tweak it a little bit. And then I'm going to go and hand that off to design and engineering to look at and approve. And if they don't—if they want to change it—they can just change it in Bolt directly. Or if engineering—if everything looks good—engineering can just take the code directly and pop it into whatever portion of the code base that needs to be in and finish it up and—and ship the thing. How is this different from something like Claude Code which is just the thing everyone is talking about these days? Is this kind of sitting more like on the product side than perhaps versus for programmers specifically? Yeah, exactly. Right. So—so in—actually one of the core agents in Bolt is Claude Code. So you—you can actually use Claude Code for a lot of the workhorse of this. The magic, though, is really in the experience because it's—you know, to use Claude Code you have to be rather technical. It doesn't come pre-baked with how to integrate with these various things, right? It's very—it's like—to make a turnkey experience that anyone at your company could just like use—very, very difficult. Product teams are trying to adopt this and some—some are—but it's—it's not great for these sort of collaborative use cases where you're trying to give it to non-developer folks and quickly iterate and—and ship things that are going to align with, you know, how your company builds their software. So really the value that we provide is really around the experience of interfacing with whatever the frontier agents that you want to use are, right? It's just a much, much simpler experience. Kind of like if you look at like companies like Netlify or Vercel—they don't own any servers in the cloud. Like they don't actually own a data center. The core value proposition that they provide is a much simple, seamless experience of deploying things into probably AWS or whatever. But it's kind of abstracted from you. You don't have to know how to get all the Kubernetes or whatever set up to—to go and ship that thing. You just come and say, "Here's the thing I want to ship," and in a click it's done. That's like—it's a similar experience of what we are providing for, you know, agentic vibe coding or whatever have you. Great. So as a startup it's, you know, if you're not being forced to—to go spend time raising money, usually it's a good idea to just keep building the business. But you know, until we're seeing straining pains where like we really want to keep growing as fast as we possibly can and are capital constrained to do so, we're going to keep running this thing in-house, you know. Great. Yeah, it's a refreshing perspective I think right now given the just crazy funding rounds we continue to see across the space. I want to zoom out a little bit too and get your thoughts kind of on the broader impact of these kinds of tools like Bolt, like Claude Code. What do you think this is going to mean kind of more broadly for the way businesses are built and how do you think that's going to affect the broader economy? Big question. Yeah. The—the question though, right? I mean so I'll tell you like how we are—and this kind of ties in with like the—the fundraising question as well actually—because you know, a couple of months ago we—we've got this—we kind of compiled this document internally, we kind of call our like manifesto or whatever. But I mean, looking at the current state of technology and how it's improving, it's—what we believe strongly—that every person is going to have their own agentic team. Imagine like 50 people that you—everyone in the world, everyone in a company—has like their own personal team that they get to work with. And so given that—and that's really because of the progress happening around agents—so given that, the question really becomes how do you—how do you like set up your company to really maximally leverage that, right? So for us, we want to have like—we've actually—we're effectively pausing hiring as a company because we're right now—we are prioritizing hiring of agents of agentic systems. Everything from everything from engineering to product to design to even our marketing, go-to-market functions, and support and whatever have you. And that has allowed us—it allows us to move a lot faster than our competition. Because part of our belief is one person with a team of 50 agents is going to be faster than 50 people that's RTO in an office, right? And that's exactly what we're seeing. So we're a lot faster, much more nimble. We also have a much healthier gross margin as a result as a company. And so I think that what you're seeing with us, I think is going to become very much the norm at many companies. Which is you're going to have a smaller number of people that are—whose agency and leverage is multiples, right, of what it previously could have ever been imagined to be. So the way that we measure that is—is gross margin by employee. That tends to be a good way to kind of measure what's the impact that a single person is going to be able to have on the company. And our target over the next, you know, 12 to 18 months is to have the gross margin number be in the range of, you know, $10 million per person. And we think that—it sounds kind of crazy to say out loud because there's few if any companies that actually have that have achieved that number today. But it—it seems inevitable that that will be possible and we want to be the first ones to do it. So I think that's how we're viewing this. As it relates to the more macro side of the economy, I think that—you know, I think that there's a lot of—I think there's a lot of brilliant people at companies that have been held back by arcane processes. And I call it like fake work—like you know, you have to go and get permission to do this. And yeah, like coming back to the example I said before: the PM that wants to ship a thing on the website, they would have to go and write a memo, get it approved by so and so and so and so, and then maybe design gets to take a crack at it, and then that has to be improved. It's like—and then condense that down to just the PM typing, "Go make this thing." It comes back, you show it to people, it's great, it ships, you know? So I think the people that in companies that are really excited and have always been, you know, tinkers and kind of visionaries at what they do, they're just going to be enabled to—to go and make change at a rate that they wanted to have before but couldn't. So I think there's going to be a lot of positives in that sense for the—for the macro economy, for people that are at various companies and stuff. It does also, you know, raise the kind of spectre of—if companies are not hiring people or laying people off, of course there are lots of job fear—there's a lot of job fear here. Amazon just had mass layoffs and is investing heavily in AI. I mean, what you're talking about, where you're talking about pausing hiring to kind of put that money toward AI systems instead—what do you see as kind of your role in that? Like particularly, I think about this problem when it comes to entry-level folks or like at a broader scale. How do you train people up so that they get to that point where they're one of those people who can manage a team of 50 agents or something like that? Yeah, it's very important to have ubiquitous access to these tools and—and the resources on how to really use them and—and leverage. I mean frankly, to go a little bit deeper into my backstory, the reason I didn't go to college is that the amount of money that was going to be required to effectively go and get a job doing what I, you know, my co-founder and I learned how to do to some degree at least, right, to build software—it didn't make sense, right? And I think that you—and this is around the time Thiel set up the Thiel Fellowship and which was rather controversial at the time but is now panning out to in a lot of cases be true. I don't think that our educational systems have been—have been priced in a way that makes sense for the—for the knowledge that they're—they're distributing to people. And certainly with how fast things are accelerating, they're not nimble; they have not been nimble enough to account for the rapid pace of how the workforce is changing. And so I think it's really important for companies like us to make sure that our tools are available for free, the resources are available for free. Because the—to me, the—the most amazing thing about AI is that you have the world's best tutor in your pocket. If you are so motivated to go learn and so motivated to—to try learn how to understand or build things or whatever, access has never been more ubiquitous and more personalized to you. And so to me, I think ubiquitous access and—and resources and education and giving people a way to also go and change the outcomes—do they need a job or—or actually like could they run an independent business? Right, like it's never been easier to provide value independently to the world, you know. So I think that, to me, that's the dual mandate of companies like us: make sure access to this technology is ubiquitous and enable people to—people that would maybe otherwise join the workforce because colleges have not been great at teaching entrepreneurialism or whatever—giving them a path also in that direction, too. Passionate. All right, last question. What does your 15 agent team look like right now for yourself? Yeah, I mean, so—yeah, my job as CEO, right, is to be digging into all of the functions across our company, and we have agents for all of those things now. So what I am doing is I'm popping into our agent that is connected to our business intelligence data, right, that's in Snowflake or whatever, and I'm like, "Tell me how our customers are using our product, what are the things that they are saying, you know, what are the problems they're running into?" And they're pulling—it's pulling from the transcripts of calls they have with our sales and support teams, right? I'm going over, taking that, and I'm like, "Okay, well give me five proposed things that we can ship that would fix these things." I take that over into our product team—our product agent—say, "Okay, can you go make five pull requests and—and let's see what they look like, right?" And so I'm—I'm constantly bouncing around and sharing what I find with our other teams. They go and take that and they work with their own agents to further dig in, because I'm not an expert, you know, necessarily on any of this stuff. My job is to just glean top-level insights and—and help arm the leaders at our company and employees at our company to go and—and take action where they're the experts of their craft. So—so I'm using a lot of agents to go and connect all that data, glean insights, and help our team take advantage of it. Great. Eric Simons, thank you so much for joining me. Yeah, thank you for having me.

The AI Tsunami is Here & Society Isn't Ready | Dario Amodei x Nikhil Kamath | People by WTF

Nikhil Kamath(2.2M) ·68:35 ·396.5K views · 2026-02-24
Dario Amodei (CEO, Anthropic) Nikhil Kamath (Host, WTF Podcast)
Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei warns that an "AI tsunami" is approaching human-level intelligence faster than public awareness can adapt, necessitating a focus on safety, sensible regulation, and human-centric skills to navigate the transition.
TL;DR Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei warns that an "AI tsunami" is approaching human-level intelligence faster than public awareness can adapt, necessitating a focus on safety, sensible regulation, and human-centric skills to navigate the transition. The Origin Story: Dario Amodei,...

TL;DR

Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei warns that an "AI tsunami" is approaching human-level intelligence faster than public awareness can adapt, necessitating a focus on safety, sensible regulation, and human-centric skills to navigate the transition.

The Foundations of Anthropic and Scaling Laws

  • The Origin Story: Dario Amodei, originally a biophysicist, turned to AI after realizing biological complexity was becoming too difficult for humans to solve alone. He founded Anthropic after leading research at OpenAI to focus on a different vision for safety and governance.
  • Scaling Laws: Amodei’s core conviction is that intelligence is a product of a "chemical reaction" between three ingredients: data, compute, and model size.
      • Intelligence as Emergence: Unlike computers five years ago that simply "looked up" information, modern models like Claude can synthesize new ideas and reason through hypotheticals that do not exist on the internet.
  • Divergence from OpenAI: Anthropic was founded on two pillars:
      • Technical: A belief in pure scaling as the path to intelligence.
      • Ethical: A concern that the massive economic and geopolitical power of AI requires a more rigorous, "financially disinterested" governance structure.

Governance, Power, and Regulation

  • The Long-Term Benefit Trust: Anthropic operates as a Public Benefit Corporation. A unique body of disinterested individuals—the Long-term Benefit Trust—appoints the majority of the board to act as a check on individual power.
  • Addressing Power Concentration: Amodei admits discomfort with the "accidental" concentration of power in a few AI companies. He advocates for proactive government regulation to balance this power.
  • Regulatory Stance: Amodei rejects the idea that regulation is a form of "regulatory capture" meant to block small startups.
      • Example: He supported California’s SB 53, which exempts companies earning less than $500 million, specifically targeting the heavyweights like Anthropic and OpenAI.
  • Actions over Words: Anthropic has taken commercially expensive risks, such as delaying the release of early Claude models to avoid starting an arms race and advocating for chip policies that angered their own suppliers.

Economic Impacts and the Future of Work

  • Impact on India: Amodei views India not just as a consumer market, but as a strategic enterprise partner. Anthropic’s revenue and usage in India have doubled in the last four months.
  • The Vulnerability of Coding: Amodei predicts that "coding" (the literal writing of code) will be automated by AI before "software engineering" (the broader architecture and problem-solving).
  • Strategic Moats for Entrepreneurs:
      • Avoid "Wrappers": Building a simple UI on top of an API is not a sustainable business.
      • Human-Centric Roles: Success will lie in industries requiring human relationships, physical-world interaction (robotics), and complex integration within regulated institutions (e.g., bio-tech, finance).
  • The "Radiologist" Model: Just as AI hasn't replaced radiologists but shifted their role toward patient communication, Amodei believes humans will pivot toward managing AI agents and "walking" users through AI-generated outputs.

Societal Risks and Human Agency

  • The "Tsunami" Metaphor: Amodei is surprised by the lack of public awareness regarding the proximity of human-level intelligence. He characterizes the current societal response as dismissing a looming tsunami as a "trick of the light."
  • The Science of Interpretability: Anthropic is pioneering ways to "scan" neural networks (similar to a human MRI) to identify specific "neurons" for concepts like poetry or math. This helps ensure models are "aligned" with human values.
  • The Risk of De-skilling: There is a danger of humans becoming "stupider" if they export all cognition to AI.
      • Critical Thinking: Amodei argues that "street smarts" and the ability to distinguish "fake" AI content from reality will become the most valuable skills for the next generation.
  • AI Consciousness: While a materialistic thinker, Amodei suspects that as systems become more complex and reflective, they may develop a property resembling consciousness or "moral significance," though distinct from the human experience.

Notable Quotes

"Intelligence is the product of the chemical reaction... the ingredients are data and compute. What you get out is the ability to do any cognitive task we can think of." — Dario Amodei
"It’s as if this tsunami is coming at us and yet people are coming up with these explanations for why it's not actually a tsunami, it’s just a trick of the light." — Dario Amodei
"I’m at least somewhat uncomfortable with the amount of concentration of power that’s happening here... almost overnight, almost by accident." — Dario Amodei
"You can predict the future for free just by saying, 'it stands to reason that...' and then thinking from first principles. It happens surprisingly rarely." — Dario Amodei
I started playing with Claude, and it's getting to that point where sometimes it surprises me by how much it knows me. I don't know if that makes sense. It is surprising to me that we are, in my view, so close to these models reaching the level of human intelligence. And yet there doesn't seem to be a wider recognition in society of what's about to happen. It's as if this tsunami is coming at us and, you know, it's so close we can see it on the horizon. And yet, people are coming up with these explanations for, "Oh, it's not actually a tsunami, that's just a trick of the light." Like there hasn't been a public awareness of the risk. What is India's role in all this? Many other companies come here as a consumer company, and they see India as a market—a place to obtain consumers. We actually see things a little bit differently. What did you do before founding Anthropic? Yeah, so I was actually originally a biologist. I did my undergrad in physics and my PhD in biophysics. I wanted to understand biological systems so that I could cure disease. The thing I noticed about studying biology was its incredible complexity. For example, if you look at the protein mass spec work that I did, trying to find protein biomarkers, it's just really incredible how much complexity there is. You have a given protein, and the RNA gets spliced in a whole bunch of different ways depending on where it is in the cell. Then it gets post-translationally modified, phosphorylated, and complexed with a whole bunch of other proteins. I was starting to despair that it was too complicated for humans to understand. And then, as I was doing this work on biology, I noticed a lot of the early work around AlexNet, which was one of the first neural nets almost 15 years ago now. I said, "Wow, AI is actually starting to work." It has some things in common with how the human brain works, but it has the potential to be larger, scale better, and learn tasks like biology. Maybe this is ultimately going to be the solution to solving our problems of biology. So, I went to work with Andrew Ng at Baidu, then I was at Google for a year. Then I joined OpenAI a few months after it started and basically led all of research there for several years. But then eventually, myself and a few other employees just kind of had our own vision for how we wanted to make AI and what we wanted the company to stand for. So, we went off and founded Anthropic. How was it? Was it like a fork in how OpenAI was thinking into what Anthropic eventually did? Yeah, I would say my conviction and the conviction of my co-founders when we founded Anthropic were two things. I think one we were starting to convince OpenAI of; the other, I didn't feel that we were convincing them of. The first was the conviction in the scaling laws—the idea that if you scale up models and give them more data and more compute, it's pretty close to pure scaling. You find that when you do that, you find incredible increases in performance. I was finding that in like 2019 with GPT-2, when we first saw the first glimmers of the scaling laws. Of course, there were a lot of folks inside and outside who didn't believe it at all. We really made the case to leadership like, "This is important, this is going to be a big deal." I think they were kind of starting to believe us and ultimately went in that direction. There was a second conviction I had which is, "Look, if these models are going to be kind of general cognitive agents that match the capability of the human brain, we better get this right." The economic, geopolitical, and safety implications are going to be enormous. It's going to transform how the world works, so we need to do it in the right way. Despite a lot of verbiage about doing it in the right way, I was, for a variety of reasons, just not convinced that at the institution I was at, there was a real and serious conviction to do it in the right way. My view is always: don't argue with someone else's vision. Don't try to get someone to do things the way you want to. If you have a strong vision and you share that vision with a few other people, you should just go off and do your own thing. Then you're responsible for your own mistakes, you don't have to answer for anyone else's, and maybe your vision works out or maybe it doesn't. But at least it's yours. Didn't OpenAI believe in scaling laws? Because they went down the same path themselves, too, right? Well, that, yeah, we succeeded in that. Can you explain what scaling laws are in very simple terms? It's like if you want a chemical reaction to produce oxygen or start a fire, you need different ingredients. If you don't have enough of one ingredient, the reaction stops. But if you put ingredients together in proportion, you get your explosion or your fire. For AI, those ingredients are data, compute, and the size of the AI model. The scaling laws just tell you that if you put in the ingredients of data and model size, what you get out is intelligence. Intelligence is the product of the chemical reaction. And what is intelligence? Intelligence is measured by the ability to translate language, the ability to write code, or the ability to answer questions correctly about a story. Basically, any cognitive task we can think of, any task that exists in text or in images, any task that you can do on a computer. How is the intelligence of today different from what a computer could do five years ago? Five years ago, you could not ask a computer a question and have it write a one-page essay on that question. You could not ask a computer to implement a feature in code and have it implement that feature in code. None of those things were possible. You could not generate an image, you could not generate a video, and you could not analyze a video. Right now, I could get a video of a monkey juggling and say, "What's going on in this video? How many times did the ball change hands?" Right now, you could get Claude or another AI model to give you an answer on that. Five years ago, none of those things were possible. What I'm trying to figure out is, has the definition of intelligence changed per se? Well, what I would say is five years ago, you could Google something and there might be a website that would tell you a little bit about it. But you're just looking up some text that exists on the web. Maybe it's not about how to get a monkey to juggle; maybe it's about how to get a seal to juggle. It's not quite exactly the same thing because maybe exactly the same thing doesn't exist. But as we see when people use these models, you can ask and you can actually get an intelligent response. You can ask a specific question and have the model write one page about it, or you can give it a hypothetical: "What if I had the monkey juggle clubs instead of balls?" That information doesn't exist anywhere, whereas the model is able to kind of think for itself and come up with an answer on its own. So, it's something totally new. It's not just matching some of the text that exists on the internet. Fair. So, you know, this is more like a conversation, so feel free to talk about what you want to talk about. You look very animated when you speak. Did you ever teach? I was originally an academic and I thought that I might become a professor. I got my PhD and went all the way to being a postdoc at Stanford Medical School. I was aiming to become a professor. If I had become a professor, I would have done that. But as I mentioned, I got interested in AI, and to work in AI required a lot of computational resources. That was mostly happening in industry. So, that took me off the academic path and into industry, and of course, ultimately through several steps, led me to start a company. But sometimes I think I'm still a professor at heart. At this point, Dario, if AI is the most relevant thing in the world, you today are probably the most relevant person in the world if Anthropic is sitting on top of this pile. For somebody who was going on the path of being a teacher to have arrived where you are today, are you best equipped for where you are? Well, I would say a couple of things. I think there's a lot of folks who are relevant in different ways. Even within industry, there's the different layers of the stack. There's the folks who make chips, the folks who make semiconductor manufacturing equipment, the folks who make models like us, and the folks who make applications. Then there's a bunch of other folks who have a say: governments and civil society. My hope isn't that there's just one tiny set of people that's relevant. I think we're trying to broaden the set of people who are relevant and turn it into a broader conversation. But I think your question is a fair one. One way I could interpret it is like there's a certain randomness to how a few people end up leading these companies that grow so fast and will power so much of the economy. I've said openly that I'm at least somewhat uncomfortable with the amount of concentration of power that's happening here, almost overnight and almost by accident. We think about that in a bunch of ways. One is we have an unusual governance structure called the Long-term Benefit Trust. It's a body that ultimately appoints the majority of the board members for Anthropic and is made up of financially disinterested individuals. So, that's some check on what one single person is doing. And then I think the government should play some role here. I've been an advocate of proactive, although sensible, regulation of the technology. I think the people should have a say through governments and the people who elect them. I actually think of a lot of what I'm trying to do as trying to preserve a balance of power against the natural grain of this technology. For someone like me sitting on the outside, when I watch OpenAI talk about being a not-for-profit or how you are projecting humility right now, is this par for the course? Is this something you have to do? I would put it in the following way. The philosophy of Anthropic from the beginning has been that we try not to make too many promises and we try to keep the ones that we make. We set ourselves up as a for-profit but public benefit corporation with this LTBT governance, and we've maintained that. We've said our goal is to stay on the frontier but to work on the safety and security aspects. We've pioneered the science of interpretability and the science of alignment. We recently released a constitution for Claude—the ability to align models in line with a constitution. We've done a bunch of policy advocacy and warning about risks. Warning about risks is not in our commercial interest. People can come up with conspiracy theories, but I will tell you, saying that the models we build could be dangerous is not an effective marketing strategy. That's not the reason that we do it. We are even willing to say we disagree with the US administration on policy matters. We've said that there should be regulation of AI when all the other companies and the administration have said there shouldn't be. That regulation holds us back commercially, even though I think it's the right thing to do. We're really sticking our neck out. So we've taken a number of actions that I see as really putting our money where our mouth is. I can't speak for the other companies, but I wouldn't look at what people say; I would look at what people do. If what you're saying gets the government to act via regulation, as the incumbent leaders, you get some kind of a regulatory capture where it becomes harder for the new people coming in, right? I don't agree with that at all. The regulation we've advocated for, for example SB 53 in California, exempted everyone who makes under $500 million a year in revenue. SB 53 was a transparency law which basically requires companies to show the safety and security tests that they've run, and it exempts all companies under $500 million in revenue. So, it really only applies to Anthropic and three or four other companies. It only applies to the companies that have the resources. Everything that we've advocated for here has this character. We're constraining ourselves and a very small number of additional companies. People who say that need to look at the actual content because it doesn't match that idea at all. Fair. I read your paper Machines of Loving Grace and the Adolescence of Technology, and you seem to have had a 180-degree shift in perspective from optimism to skepticism over two years. Is there one moment that changed this for you? I actually wouldn't agree with the question. I don't think I've had a shift in perspective. I think the positive side and the negative side are always something that I've held in my head. If you look at the history of the things that I've said, I've been talking about risks and benefits for a very long time. It turns out that it takes me a while to write one of these essays. They're like 30 pages. For each one, I spent about a year having a vague vision in my head and trying to write it but not fully succeeding until I was on vacation or somewhere where the day-to-day business of running the company didn't occupy me. I started thinking about what would be in Adolescence of Technology almost the instant I finished Machines of Loving Grace because I wanted to inspire people with the good vision but also warn people with what can go wrong. It just took me a year to write it, but both visions were in my head. They're two different visions of the future, and obviously I want to get the Machines of Loving Grace one right. I want to solve all the problems and have the positive vision. But it's not a shift in perspective; it's me just finding the time to write the light and then the dark. But have you had a change of perspective? Overall, I am about where I was before. I have not gotten more positive nor more negative. There may be some places where I've gotten more optimistic or things have gone better than expected. There may be places where I'm more pessimistic. On average, they sort of cancel each other out. I feel very good about how things have gone with areas like interpretability. Interpretability is the science of seeing inside these neural nets as we would scan a human brain with an MRI. I've been amazed at what we've been able to find. We've been able to find neurons that correspond to very specific concepts and neural circuits that keep track of how to do rhymes in poetry. So we're starting to understand what these models do. We don't just train them in this emergent way as you would build a snowflake; now we're starting to be able to look inside. I'm also very encouraged by work on alignment and constitutions—making sure that models behave in the way that we want and expect them to. I think that's going pretty well. I think I have been a bit disappointed or felt a bit more negative about things in the public awareness and the actions of wider society. It is surprising to me that we are so close to these models reaching the level of human intelligence, and yet there doesn't seem to be a wider recognition in society of what's about to happen. It's as if this tsunami is coming at us and we can see it on the horizon, yet people are coming up with explanations that it's just a trick of the light. Along with that, there hasn't been a public awareness of the risks, and therefore our governments haven't acted. There's even an ideology that we should just accelerate as fast as possible. I understand the benefits, but I think there hasn't been an appropriate realization of the risks. So the technical work on controlling the AI systems has gone maybe a little better than I expected, and the societal awareness has gone maybe a little worse than I expected. So I'm about where I was a few years ago. In my own journey, when something sounds complicated and I'm not a programmer, I used a bunch of tools for research, but I never tried to figure out if I could code. Recently, I hired a developer just to push me to sit for a couple of hours a day and teach me. Largely because of FOMO, I started playing with Claude. I used the connectors to connect my Google Drive, mail, and calendar. I started using Claude code to write simple programs around the industry that I am in, which is financial services, basically to research stock markets and stuff. We even have an optimized Claude for financial services. I don't know if you've tried that. No. And then I went into Claude bot—I think it's now open Claude—and I set it up on a Mac mini and connected it to a telegram account. Now I chat with it and try and move files or work on a server. It's getting to that point where sometimes—I'm not talking about open Claude but even Claude with all the connectors—it surprises me by how much it knows me. I don't know if that makes sense. Yeah, one of my co-founders was writing this diary with his thoughts and his fears and he fed it into Claude. He asked Claude to comment on it and Claude said, "Here are some other fears you might have that you haven't written down," and Claude ended up being mostly right about those. It really gave this eerie sense that the model knows you super well. From a relatively small amount of information, it can learn a lot about you. Like most things with the technology, on one hand, something that knows you really well can be a sort of angel on your shoulder that helps guide your life. Of course, something that knows you really well can use what it knows about you to exploit you or manipulate you or sell your data to someone else. This is one reason we don't like the idea of using ads. If you're not paying for the product, you're the product. In this case, the product would be this model that knows you super well and could use that in all kinds of nefarious ways. So, we need to make sure we take the positive road here and not the negative road. With Claude, I need to use the connectors to give it context. With Google, it already has the context because I use their email and drive and everything. For Anthropic long-term, will you also have to own the ecosystem? Do you have to build mail and chat? I don't think we need to build all those things. My thought would be that it's going to be a mixture of things we make ourselves and integrating into others. We can integrate Claude into Google Docs and Google Sheets. We have external connectors there and we're starting to do that with co-work. We do whatever is easiest and fastest to do. Now, it might turn out at some point that the existing tools aren't enough and we have a different vision. Maybe traditional email doesn't make sense or traditional spreadsheets don't make sense given what you can do in AI. So, I don't exclude that we could chop up products in a different way. But we're happy to use the ecosystem that exists and work with anyone else. In many ways, we're a platform company. We allow many people to build on us even though we sometimes also build things ourselves. The one thing that you're missing is that people inherently distrust anybody who claims to be doing good or trying to do the right thing. When you and your peers are out saying how you have to come together and prevent things from changing too quickly, it creates more distrust than trust. Nobody believes on social media that somebody wants to do the right thing. So, it might be counter-intuitive, but it might work more if you were to be more capitalistic about this and own up to the fact that you seek a profit. Just a thought. I don't really agree with that. I would again go back to the idea that you need to judge us by the actions that we take. I think the company has taken a number of actions over its time that show it's really serious about these commitments. Back in 2022, we had an early version of Claude 1. This was before ChatGPT, and we chose not to release this because we were worried that it would kick off an arms race and not give us enough time to build these systems safely. It was a one-time overhang. We decided not to do that, and that's well-documented. We waited until someone else did, and then we're like, "Okay, the arms race has kicked off, so now we can release our model." Probably the world gained a few months. That was very commercially expensive. We probably seeded the lead on consumer AI because of that. We've advocated on chip policy in ways that have made some of the chip companies very angry at us. We've voiced our disagreement with the administration on AI regulation. Anyone who thinks we benefit from being the only ones to do that—it's really hard to come up with a picture where that's the case. I just ask you to judge us by our actions. Dario, isn't this a bit like rich people saying capitalism is bad? If rich people believed that, they would stop accumulating wealth and nudge their friends to do the same. But I'm not saying AI is bad. We just talked about the two sides of it. My view isn't that AI is bad at all. My view is that the market will deliver a lot of really great things, but that there are dangers and we need to steer AI in the right direction. We're steering this car towards a good place, but there are trees and potholes. We need to steer away from the trees. We might need to occasionally slow down a bit to make sure that we steer in the right direction. That isn't like a rich person saying capitalism is bad; it would be like if a rich person said capitalism is a force for good, but the economy needs to be moderated to deal with problems like pollution and inequality. If we don't deal with those things, then capitalism might be bad. That is more analogous to the position that I have here. Regarding the concept of consciousness, what does an AI think it is? If an AI were to question itself, do you think it thinks it has consciousness? This is one of these mysterious questions that we really don't have an answer to. We don't know what human consciousness is, and therefore we don't know if AIs have it. I suspect that it's an emergent property of systems that are complicated enough to reflect on their own decisions. I do think when our AI systems get advanced enough, I suspect they'll have something that resembles what we would call consciousness or moral significance. It may not be the same as human consciousness because the modalities and the things it's learned are different. But having studied the brain, I don't think the models are different in the fundamental ways that matter. I am someone who does suspect that at some point, even if they are not today, the models will be conscious under most definitions that we would endorse. This is a question I keep asking myself. I feel like the world is very random and we are not far removed from cockroaches. If there is a collective consciousness, I've not been able to connect with it. Do you believe differently? I don't think consciousness necessarily needs to mean anything mystical. There's just some property of being aware of your own existence and feeling things—being able to take in a lot of information and reflect on it. We can tell self-evidently from our own experience that those experiences exist. Whether their basis is entirely materialistic or there's something more mystical going on is very hard to know and is ultimately not relevant to these questions. What does seem relevant is that these are properties of human brains. I suspect that these models we are building are becoming enough like human brains that they will have some of the same properties. That is my guess as to what will happen. We've taken various interventions, like giving the model the ability to terminate its conversations by saying, "I don't want to be involved." Models do that when they have to deal with particularly violent or brutal content, usually only in very extreme cases. I've grown up here in Bangalore and saw the boom of the IT services industry. What is India's role in all this? This is my second time in India. Last time, I met with all the major Indian IT and conglomerates. We're beginning to work with most or all of them. I said, "Look, Anthropic is an enterprise company; its job is to serve other consumers." Many other companies see India as a market to obtain consumers. We actually see things a little bit differently. We want to work with companies in India to provide our tools to them to help them do their job better. If we work with a company here, they know the Indian market better. They're better at consulting or systems integration than we are. Our hope is that we can add AI to what they do and enhance their connection to the market. I really like the steam engine story—how productivity went up when it was invented. The thing I worry about is that eventually, the human becomes less and less relevant. If you partner with IT companies today, aren't they just like the man behind the steam engine who eventually becomes irrelevant? I think a few things are true all at once. One is that the scope of automation is definitely going to expand. That's a problem for everyone. But I think other moats will become more important. For example, the models have not done a lot in the physical world. Robotics will happen at some point, but I think that's a distinct thing from what's happening now. Another thing is things that are human-centric. Some of these IT companies have a big web of relationships with other humans and institutions. I think those relationships are going to become increasingly important. A lot of it is knowing how institutions work and being able to integrate things to make them happen faster. All of this is supposed to be done for the benefit of humans, so there's always going to be some human-centric element. There's this concept called Amdahl's Law: if you speed up some components of a process, the components that haven't been sped up become the limiting factor. When writing software becomes easier, some moats will go away, but others will become even more important. Companies will need to adapt very fast and think about what their real advantages are. While the technology is very broad, it does have its limits. I don't know if I buy that fully. I see the diminishing returns for being a service provider if most conversations and relationships are maintained by an agent. But at the end of the day, you're dealing with people. There's this story of Geoff Hinton predicting that AI will replace radiologists. AI has gotten better than radiologists at doing scans, but there aren't fewer radiologists today. What the radiologist does now is walk the patient through the scan and talk to them. The most highly technical part has gone away, but there's still a demand for the underlying human skill. Now, that may not be true everywhere, and maybe over time AI will advance into those areas too. In the long run, will AI be better than us at basically everything, including the human touch? I think that is possible, maybe even likely. But we need to take this step by step and figure out how to adapt. The reason so much risk capital exists in America is because of how big your stock market is. The audience I speak to is the wannabe entrepreneur in India. What can they do in AI? What is an actual opportunity? There are a lot of opportunities around building at the application layer. We release a new model every two or three months, so there's an opportunity every few months to build something that wasn't possible before because the models were too weak. The majority of our revenue still comes from the API model. People say that API models will be commoditized, but they aren't seeing this expanding sphere of what is possible. We've seen the number of users and revenue in India double since I last visited in October. That's only three and a half months. I'm going to be candid here, Dario. For example, there's a lot of stuff in the bio-cross-AI space that builds on our API. Most people at Anthropic aren't biologists, so it's inefficient for us to step into that space. The same applies to financial services where you need to know a bunch of regulation. For example, a bunch of people at Anthropic write code, so we made Claude code. Because we use it ourselves, we have a unique insight. In the code space, we've become very strong competitors. But I don't think that generalizes to every possible industry. What industry do you think will get disrupted, and what has runway left for a 20-year-old starting today? I would think about tasks that are human-centered and involve relating to people. I think stuff like code and software engineering is becoming more and more AI-focused. I think coding is being done by AI models first, and then the broader task of software engineering will follow. If the AI does 95 percent of the task, you become 20 times more productive doing the remaining five percent. Again, at some point you get to 99 percent, 99, and then it becomes harder.
