Episode 50: Ian Livingstone - Co-Founder of London Regional Properties

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.7K) ·31:12 ·52 views · 2026-02-24
Ian Livingstone (Co-founder, London Regional Properties)
Ian Livingstone, co-founder of London Regional Properties, built a $10B AUM real estate empire by executing a "contracyclical" strategy—buying distressed assets when institutions flee—and prioritizing direct operational control over passive ownership.
TL;DR Ian Livingstone, co-founder of London Regional Properties, built a $10B AUM real estate empire by executing a "contracyclical" strategy—buying distressed assets when institutions flee—and prioritizing direct operational control over passive ownership. The "Entrepreneurial...

TL;DR

Ian Livingstone, co-founder of London Regional Properties, built a $10B AUM real estate empire by executing a "contracyclical" strategy—buying distressed assets when institutions flee—and prioritizing direct operational control over passive ownership.

From Optical Retail to Real Estate Titan

  • The "Entrepreneurial Professional" Path: Originally trained as an optometrist, Livingstone opened his first eyewear store in 1989. Within three years, he acquired the David Clulow chain, growing it to 170 stores before selling to Luxottica in 2011.
  • Capitalizing on Crises: During the 1990-1991 financial downturn, Livingstone expanded his retail footprint by leveraging landlord desperation.
      • Incentives: He secured two-year rent-free periods and capital for fit-outs, meaning many stores cost nearly nothing to open.
      • Efficiency: He succeeded by being "nimble" and "quick to change products" in a market dominated by inefficient, slow-moving groups.
  • The 1987 Pivot: Livingstone and his brother Richard founded London Regional Properties during a commercial property crash.
      • Contrarian Conviction: While institutions were exiting, they bought assets at 50% discounts, convinced that panic selling had caused massive mispricing.
      • Leverage: Because they lacked significant capital, they relied on smaller banks willing to provide high loan-to-value (LTV) ratios on distressed assets.

Investment Philosophy: The "Two Pockets" Strategy

  • Contracyclical Timing: Livingstone’s core strategy is simple: "Buy when the institutions are selling and sell when they are buying."
  • Asset Segmentation: He manages the portfolio through two mental "pockets":
      • Trading Stock: Assets bought to be turned around and resold for a profit within a few years.
      • Family Office/Safe Havens: Long-term trophy assets (like the Fairmont Monte Carlo or Cliveden House) held for future generations. These prioritize capital appreciation and stability over immediate high yield.
  • Finding the "Non-Institutional" Gap: In the early years, the firm specialized in niches that pension funds and public companies ignored, such as sale-and-leaseback models for "mission-critical" real estate (cinemas, petrol stations, sports clubs).
  • Operational Risk: While the firm initially sought long-term leases to avoid risk, they evolved to embrace operational risk. They now prefer buying unbranded or "untried" hotels because they understand how to manage the underlying business performance.

Direct Operations and the "Cody" Platform

  • Eliminating Agency Conflict: Unlike many investors who use third-party managers (e.g., Marriott or Hilton), London Regional operates most of its properties directly through its "Cody" management platform.
  • Alignment of Interests: Livingstone argues that big hotel brands prioritize their own shareholders and fee generation over the property owner’s bottom line.
      • Control: Direct management allows for faster decision-making regarding staffing, food and beverage brands, and CapEx.
      • CapEx Tensions: He notes that franchised brands often demand "Property Improvement Plans" (PIPs) that are inconsistent with a specific hotel’s business model.
  • Institutional Partnership: While the firm has remained 100% family-funded for 40 years, they are now open to partnering with institutional capital as the "operating partner," provided they can maintain a significant equity stake (20–50%).

Current Market Outlook and Global Scale

  • Market Stance: Livingstone is currently "neutral to slightly negative" on the global property market. He is "sitting on his hands" regarding major acquisitions, citing significant headwinds and a lack of conviction in any specific asset class at current pricing.
  • Panama Pacifico: One of the firm’s most ambitious projects is a $2B–$3B development in Central America.
      • Strategic Bet: He views Panama as a "safe haven" in the region due to the US dollar, transparent rule of law, and its status as a beneficiary of neighboring instability.
  • Building a Team: To maintain an opportunistic edge, Livingstone emphasizes hiring "curious" people and remaining hands-on. He and his brother still speak to senior management daily and personally vet ideas.

Notable Quotes

"If you did nothing else but buy when the institutions were selling and sell when they were buying, you could make a very good living. It wasn’t much more complicated than that." — Ian Livingstone
"Luck is when you see an opportunity and grab it. Find things that you like doing... Don’t worry about the returns short-term, do what is your passion because you will end up being good at it." — Ian Livingstone
"The whole hotel management contract model is not really aligned with ownership very well... In many cases, the hotel owner was coming second or even third after the guest services." — Ian Livingstone
This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Ian Livingstone, co-founder of London Regional Properties, one of the largest and most respected private investors in Europe. Ian and his brother have spent four decades building quietly and deliberately, letting the results speak for themselves. This is only his second podcast ever. Ian grew up in Ealing, London, as the son of a dentist. He studied optometry, qualified in 1984, and five years later opened his first eyewear store. Within three years, he'd acquired the David Clulow chain and grown it to 170 stores, including concessions in Harrods and Selfridges, eventually selling to Luxottica in 2011. But the business he spent four decades quietly building is something else entirely. In 1987, at the height of the commercial property crash, Ian and his brother founded London Regional, buying distressed assets at half price while every institution was running for the exits. That contrarian bet became the foundation of a portfolio that today includes David Lloyd Leisure, the Hilton Hotels in London, the leasehold on Cliveden House, a $2 billion development in Panama City, and the Fairmont Monte Carlo, among others. In our conversation, we talked about going from optometrist to entrepreneur, the conviction it took to buy when everyone else was selling, four decades of family control, how he sees opportunity across wildly different asset classes, and what the market looks like to him right now. This episode also marks a milestone: episode 50 of Generating Alpha. Thank you to everyone who has followed along since the beginning. As always, if you've enjoyed this episode, please follow the podcast, rate it five stars on Spotify, subscribe on YouTube, and share it with anyone who you think might find it valuable. I really enjoyed talking to Ian, and I hope you guys enjoy listening to this conversation. Thank you. Thanks Ian for coming on, I really appreciate it. Okay, good. Fire away. Well, I want to start where I always do, at the beginning. If I'm correct, you grew up in Ealing, London, the son of a dentist. Tell me a little bit about your childhood, what that environment was like, and how you think that might have shaped you. I think, you know, most of growing up, most of our friends came from families where they were middle-class professionals. And that was the assumption: that you became a professional. So you became a doctor, or a dentist, or a lawyer, or an accountant. And so the school really geared you towards careers and it was very linear. Eventually, I would say that probably half of them stayed in those careers and half moved out, some into business aligned to that or something fairly close on. So the expectation was that you did much of the same. And if I'm correct, you started off in the business, or in optometry at City University, qualified in 1984. Again, as part of being a professional, I was sort of told "what do you want to do?" and I decided that medicine was a bit too long of a slog, even though a lot of my friends and family were all doctors. I decided that optometry was better because ultimately you could have a business as well. And so that was how I started. And in 1989, if I'm correct, you opened your first optometry store, Optica. Yeah, I worked for other people and then I bought my first store in '89. Then I started expanding it in 1990 and 1991. And yeah, in three years you'd acquired David Clulow and were running a chain worth around 20 million. Tell me about those early days. How did you decide to expand? What was the expansion looking like? Well, I never had much capital, and so it was a question of eking out the cash flow, managing your suppliers, your creditors, and borrowing a little bit of money from the bank. But I kept borrowings to an absolute minimum because I didn't really want to be overleveraged. In those days, you could get stores because there was a bit of a financial crisis—I seem to remember one of many—but in those days, landlords had a load of empty shops and they would give you quite big incentives. So you could end up with a package of a year's or two years' free rent and some capital towards your fit-out. It really, in many cases, cost nothing really to get the store open. And then if you traded profitably early days, then that was a great business. So for a while, we enjoyed the benefits of having the landlords on side. Of course, as the economy picked up, then those incentives evaporated and you had to go and compete with everybody else. But overall, I have to say, it was a good time to be in business because the business was dominated by some groups that were not really that efficient. If you could be nimble in business, if you could be quick to change your products, quick to offer different services, and you could differentiate yourself, it was quite an easy time to expand the business. You couldn't do that today; it would be much more difficult. And how did you guys differentiate yourself and why did you eventually, at the beginning, decide to build a business instead of becoming a pure practitioner? I think it was exciting opening a business, actually. I think it was exciting, it was challenging, it was fun, and it was interesting. And don't forget, I'd worked several years for other people and therefore I knew who all the best staff were. They wanted to come on; you could incentivize people to move quite easily. So we built up a really, really good management team very quickly. And that helped us expand because I knew immediately who would be the managers of the next stores as we opened. We grew our own talent, we trained people, and I was very, very hands-on in the beginning of that business. Very hands-on. I knew everybody, I knew what was going on, and I knew all the turnovers of the stores every single day. Of course, as it got bigger, I became a bit more remote from it and then I started working with my brother in real estate. But the foundations of the business were that really, which was about customer service, about being adept, being quick to change, and not being stuck in a particular business model. And it was fun. Yeah, and a few years before that, during '87, during the commercial property crash, you and your brother founded London Regional Properties. And everyone else was running for the exits, you guys had conviction to go up and buy a property in a brand new industry. How did you take advantage of that situation? You had some capital from the business, but how did you really take off? We had a little bit. We didn't have a huge amount of capital, but at the time we found one or two lenders that were prepared to back us who believed that long-term we would be right and that prices would recover. Property, at the end of the day, to some extent is a leverage game. If you can't put leverage in real estate, you can't make really serious returns on property. And so if you had no leverage, we couldn't have done it. But at the time, we found one or two smaller banks that were prepared to back us, giving us a decent amount of loan to buy distressed properties. And then of course your equity multiplies very, very quickly if you've got debt. And what gave you the conviction as someone who wasn't necessarily in the business before, and new to it, to go the complete opposite way that everyone else was going? My brother was at the time, I think he started at Richard Ellis before it was CBRE, and he'd been in the investment market. He'd seen the values, and we could just see distress. When you see things where prices have halved, then I think that you look at it and you think, well, there's something wrong here. Either the market is going to collapse completely and tenant demand's going to dry up, or you think it's going to come back. And we were fairly convinced it would come back and things were mispriced because of panic selling, really. And through the high level, I'd love for you to walk me through the evolution of London Regional Properties from '87 until today. What's been the biggest milestones or crises that most shaped what you guys are today? I think one of our big businesses was the sale and leaseback model. And we did many transactions, without going into details, but we did most of the big sale and leasebacks in Europe for several years. So that was a great business for us. We had what we called at the time triple-net leases and mission-critical real estate very often where the business took place in the asset. So there were things like that, that was a strategy. We got into hotels quite early, and we started to understand operational risk quite early. We did a bunch of deals, PPP deals with the government. Again, we understood the transfer of risk when institutions weren't prepared to do those kind of deals because they weren't institutional. I think you have to remember the backdrop of all this was that real estate in those days was a business controlled by pension funds, large public property companies, and PE was minimal. There weren't really any big PE funds in real estate in those days. And so if it wasn't institutional, like sale and leasebacks or hotels or whatever, the big public companies and the institutions, the life funds, all those sort of things were just not interested. So you could create value, you could find value very easily. Now every niche is being picked over by a fund who's deploying usually other people's money, desperate to get money out the door, earn fees for AUM, and it's not clear with all of them that they're going to be successful. Some will be very successful, others won't. And so how has your approach to making money developed over time as that kind of evolved? It was just, we were contracyclical. If you did nothing else but buy when the institutions were selling and sell when they were buying, you could make a very good living. It wasn't much more complicated than that. Then it evolved into buying niche things that they weren't interested in. As I said, it could have been hotels, it could have been doing a sale and leaseback of a, I don't know, a sports club or a cinema or a petrol station or something. They didn't get it; they just said "that's not institutional, it doesn't fit into our bucket." Now, of course, it's very different because private equity, VC, whatever you want to call it, you know, they're looking for returns and they'll buy that stuff. So the market's much more crowded than it was and it's harder to differentiate yourself. And one of those specific hotel deals was the Fairmont Monte Carlo which you guys bought in 2007, one of the more iconic hotels in the world. I'd love for you to tell me the story behind that purchase and kind of how you've built it over the years. Not a great deal to say about it. You know, it was owned by a consortium that included Prince, what's his name, the Saudi Prince. The Monaco one? No, no, no, no, no, no, no. The consortium that owned it was the Saudi Prince, Al-Waleed. Al-Waleed bin Talal. So he owned it in a consortium with Fairmont and some private equity. Anyway, they rebranded it to Fairmont. And they just wanted to resell it because it was a short-term play for them, a long-term play for us. So we bought it from them, there was no great secret. We spent recently a lot of money refurbishing the hotel, it's doing well, and we're very happy with it. And it's, I live in Monaco and it's nice to own a big hotel here; it's one of the biggest in this part of the Med. And you just mentioned something that the Saudis were trading versus you guys kind of had long-term approach to. And you've talked about distinguishing between those two kinds of assets for yourself. How do you think about that split and what moves something from one category to another? I think as we develop our business, we have, in my mind—not physically, but in my mind—we have two pockets. And the first pocket is deals that we buy which we don't think we want to own very long-term, but we think that there's a way to turn them around and resell them or sell them in a few years and make a profit. That's kind of trading stock and that's what most property companies do. But given the fact that, you know, we've been reasonably successful, we look at some assets as assets for our family office, for our kids, for the future, which are very long-term. We can take much lower returns on those because we're in it for the very long-term and it doesn't really matter whether, you know, the income is up or down over the next five years because you're looking at capital value. I'm just going to go and sit here where it's a bit quieter. You're looking at capital value appreciation or safe-haven assets. So I think that if we ended up with a bunch of safe-haven prime assets for our family office, that's a good place to be. Before we go back to the episode, I want to take a short break to talk about my sponsor, Rho. The Generating Alpha podcast is presented by Rho, the all-in-one banking platform for startups. Thousands of startups like Perplexity, Product Hunt, and more use Rho. You get everything you need to manage your startup's cash: fast banking setup, cards with up to 2% cashback, and yield that turns company cash into extra runway. All super important in the early days of launching. But the thing founders really love about Rho is their team. They are obsessed with helping founders disrupt the status quo and will go to the end of the earth to help them do so. And exclusively for Generating Alpha podcast listeners and viewers, you'll get a $1,500 statement credit plus a ton of exclusive perks when you manage your company cash with Rho. Terms and conditions apply. To learn more, visit RHO.co/generatingalpha. Rho is a fintech, not a bank. Checking and card services provided by Webster Bank, Member FDIC. See reward terms for details. Thank you, and back to the episode. And over time, you've kept the firm, if I'm correct, entirely within family control. No outside capital for nearly four decades. Was that deliberate from the start or did it just happen that way? No, it happened that way. I think there were many times when we wanted to get external capital because we saw deals that were really interesting. Every time we tried, because we were a bit contracyclical or a bit ahead of the curve, people looked at those deals and said, "no, no, no, those are the wrong things." We could never raise third-party capital in those days. So we had to do it ourselves. We pushed it and we reinvested a lot. We saved up, you know, we were very careful with what we did, and we did deals ourselves and we made decent money out of doing it. But in many cases, we would have taken third-party investors in had they believed in it. But they didn't believe in it, and then they would come back six months after we'd done the deal and say, "oh, can we look at that now?" and we'd say, "no, we can't." That was the story of what happened. It wasn't that we didn't want to; we couldn't. We were too entrepreneurial, I think, which is a weird thing to say but it was very much the case. I mean, now we can, but we've got our own capital. It's not to say that we won't partner up with institutional capital on some deals, and I think that's the next phase for us. We've built this amazing platform, Cody, that works for us, that runs our hotel business and has built a sensational hotel management platform, which is probably best in class. And we now will partner perhaps with more institutional capital on some of the very big deals and we'll be the operating partner. But uniquely for an operating partner, we're prepared to put serious equity into a deal, and that could be 20, 30, or 50%, whereas most operating partners want fees, fees, fees and don't put any equity in. So that's it. Sorry, I was saying you just mentioned operating properties and you guys operate, if I'm correct, most of your properties directly. I'm interested just what does owning and operating give you rather than just owning passively? It gives us much, much better control over the P&L. It gives us control over the staff and the quality of the asset. It means that we haven't got an operating company whose aims may diverge from that of the owner. In other words, what a big hotel management company wants is to maximize the value for its shareholders, to maximize its fees, and not necessarily to maximize the bottom line for the hotel owner. So I felt that in many cases, the hotel owner was coming second or even third after the guest services. We've had instances of that where the whole hotel management contract model is not really aligned with ownership very well. Therefore, if you can own it and keep flexibility, then you can decide much more quickly how you want to operate it and how you want to change things. You can be more nimble, you can be more flexible on bringing in other food and beverage brands, for example, or changing the staff, or managing it differently. Whereas if you're part of a big, thousand-hotel-plus chain, it's very hard to be nimble and change things. And let's say you buy a franchised hotel and they hand you a CapEx list that's inconsistent with what you guys want. How do you navigate that tension? You don't buy it. Yeah. And so what happens is the seller is getting screwed because it's going to come pound-for-pound off his pricing. Your ability to manage that PIP depends on your relationship with the brand. So if I owned 50 Marriotts, I've got a better chance of negotiating something sensible than if I've got two Marriotts, or Hiltons, or whatever. So that CapEx, it's a big issue, that PIP, because I understand from the brand's perspective there's their checklist, but it's not always consistent with the business model of that individual hotel. And you know, it needs to be proportional and it isn't always. It can be and in some cases it's not. And that can be a big detractor of value in franchised hotels. You mentioned that that was part of the kind of diligence process. What does the entire diligence process look like for you guys from a high level? What are some key things you evaluate? We evaluate the strength of the market—you know, what are the drivers, how much is business, how much is leisure, how much is seasonal. We evaluate what's the pipeline of new product coming into the market, and what's the five-year look forward and look back on occupancy. Can we operate it? We look at the ability to hire and fire staff, the flexibility of labor. Can we get labor? And if we get labor that's wrong, can we move people out, can we swap people, get rid of them? How strong are the unions? How helpful are the unions? In some cases they're very helpful and in other cases they're very unhelpful. We look at what the economic forecast is like for that country or city. We look at key drivers like conference centers, airports, arenas—all those sort of things. There's many, many things that you've got to take into account. You know, we've seen lots of things recently. We saw one the other day where it was an airport at a capital city hotel in Europe, but not a major capital, not a major city. It was a sort of a B-location for the airport, but it was fairly solid. And it was great until we found out there was a 500-room terminal-linked hotel opening within three years in a small market. Which was the end of that deal. And you've done deals across different sectors in real estate, so retail, hospitality, but you've also done some tech deals and things across the place. Is there a unifying lens in which you see opportunity or do you look at each opportunity in and of itself? We've always been opportunistic. When people bring things to us, we've got the gut... My brother or I usually has a gut feeling whether it's something that we want to invest in. And of course what you're looking at in these investments is risk-return. So, if you can find something that's limited risk with a decent return, you're going to do it. But if the risk-return scenario is wrong (and that's what happens in real estate in some markets), you find out there's huge competition, there's very little margin, and there's downside. So you have to learn to sit on your hands at those points and say, "this market's not for me." I'll either find another market or I'll just stay out completely if you're not seeing pricing where you can make money because there's always risk. And on the topic of risk, how has your relationship with risk evolved from '87 until now? Have you become more comfortable taking risk because it's stayed the same, or were you always as comfortable? No, I think where we've got really good properties, be they hotel or commercial, if they are not leased long-term or they've not got long-term income, we're much more capable now, or more sophisticated, at taking a view about either re-letting or performance going forward. Whereas when we first started with hotels, we started out with leases because we couldn't really understand the operational risk. Now we understand the operational risk, we're happy to buy something that's unbranded or untried. We're happy to buy an office building if it's got different lease lengths because if we think that it's fundamentally a great play, we'll buy it. So we've got more sophisticated underwriting things. When we started, it was all about getting long leases. And one of your more unique projects right now is Panama Pacifico. How does a firm like you guys end up developing $700 million worth of real estate in Central America? It's probably about 2 billion or 3 billion by the time this is finished. But well, there's a couple of things. Number one, I have a great partner who's been my partner in it for getting on for 15 years, who's been incredibly supportive and has great contacts locally. So that's a big plus, having a good partner. And we've built a proper management team there. We don't try and manage it remotely from London or from anywhere or New York. We believe in Panama as a destination; we believe in it as a good place to do business: transparent, decent rule of law, US dollar, and a beneficiary from what I would call unstable countries in the neighborhood. Long-term, we think everything aligns for Panama. We've had our challenges there with infrastructure being delivered late and things, but that's normal for that part of the world. But it's a sensational project, an amazing landbank, and it will do very well for the foreseeable future. So very happy with that. But it's not something that everybody would have done. For sure. And to that point, you've mentioned the idea of being opportunistic many times. How do you make sure this firm stays opportunistic, is willing to adapt, and is willing to take risk rather than stay complacent with the foundation you guys have built up now? That's a very good question. I think we have to employ people that are curious, that are motivated to do interesting things. And I think apart from picking your right management team, I think being directly involved every day in the business, which I am and my brother is, matters. We speak to our senior management every day. We send ideas, we bombard them with clips from newspapers or things that we've heard about or chatting to people. So it's staying... How do you stay relevant? You read, you meet people, you think about stuff. Having a great management team has probably enabled us to look around a little bit more for ideas. And are you currently sitting on your hands in property? Are you currently going out and buying? What's your view on the market right now? Neutral to slightly negative in general. I think that if we want to buy, I have one or two convictions that I'm looking at at the moment which I would want to do in a fairly big way. I haven't pulled the trigger on anything right now. We're buying individual assets here and there, but not enough to really move the needle. So we're buying ones and twos, be it commercial real estate, be it hotels. Opportunistically, we're buying one here, one there, one here, one there. In order to do something significant, we'd have to find something of scale that we could roll out. Let's say we decided that we wanted to buy, for argument's sake, warehousing in Eastern Europe. We would build a team and try and do it in a very big way very quickly. I have one final question for you because we're approaching the end of our time that I ask every single one of my guests. I'm 16 today. Let's stick to the business side of it for now. We'll be here a long time if I want to give you personal relationship advice, and I'm not sure I'm the best person to do that.
