"I found my voice at a later stage... The only thing I do regret is when I didn't say things I should have said at the right time. Don't think about it... you have to take the opportunity. Tomorrow you might not have it." — Ayumi Moore Aoki
Welcome to Meet the Leader, the podcast where top leaders share how they're tackling the world's toughest challenges. In today's episode, we talk to Ayumi Moore Aoki of Women in Tech Global about patching the leaky pipeline in tech and science for women's careers. Subscribe to Meet the Leader on Apple, Spotify, and wherever you get your favorite podcasts. And don't forget to rate and review us. I'm Linda Lacina from the World Economic Forum, and this is Meet the Leader. Only 2.6% of global funding went to all-women teams. It's good to have accelerators, and yes, it's good to have mentoring and to have training, but they need funding. It will take another 123 years to bridge the gender gap, according to our latest edition of the World Economic Forum Global Gender Gap Report. At the same time, women are missing out on careers in the AI sector, and they're more likely to lose their jobs to generative AI. What is needed to fix that? Ayumi Moore Aoki has some thoughts. She is the founder of Women in Tech Global. That is an organization headquartered in Paris that operates in over 60 countries with more than 250,000 members all around the world, all to boost opportunity and access and drive skill development in science and technology. I talked with her this May at the World Economic Forum Global Technology Retreat in San Francisco. She told me about the leaky pipeline in tech that exists at every stage in a woman's career. It makes it harder to get and retain women in these fields, but she also shared what leaders can do to counteract this. She shared with me where progress is being made and how our AI-powered world could present a special opportunity for women leaders. She believes they can thrive provided they learn to leverage both their tech-savvy and their empathy. She'll talk about all of that as well as her personal journey, from her early days in South Africa during apartheid to the simple piece of advice she got when she founded Women in Tech. Advice that can help anyone move faster towards a big goal. We'll get into all of that, but first she'll share the state of women in tech. So, the state of women in tech today is quite promising. We are up two points from 26% two years ago to 28%. However, we're still less than one-third of the workforce. That means that we still have a lot of room for improvement. What is going great with that? What are the areas where we're seeing a lot of success? Are there certain fields where we're seeing success or certain regions? "The science field is one that is really going up. Whether it's in AI, in research, or in science, we have many, many more women scientists coming to the place. This is a great thing. In tech, however, especially in software and hard tech, women are still very much underrepresented, including in AI." Our Gender Parity in the Intelligent Age report, which came out this spring, found that there's an uneven situation happening in certain ecosystems. This means there are clusters in one region that may not be happening in another, creating an imbalance across different economies. What problem can that cause? "Well, we have the problem for the region itself because they're going to be struggling to have women represented in a certain area. However, what I do find positive is that we can really learn from the different areas in economies around the world. We can see what is working in that region and how they managed to get there." "Then we can really exchange best practices. For example, if you look at the Middle East right now, in North Africa and Middle East countries, women graduates in STEM fields are gender equal. In some countries, there are even more women graduates than men. So you can see there's a lot of progress in that sense." "Something that is not true when you look at the European graduates or also in North America. You look at France; about 22% of women graduates in Europe are in France. We can learn best practices from them. The difficulty is converting these graduate students into the workforce." "So we see that in Europe and in the US as well, and also in Latin America, there is this conversion which is happening where they are going into the tech workforce where they still represent less. However, in the Middle East where there's a lot of graduates in the fields, they don't convert into the workforce." "So you see that there's a leaky pipeline at very different stages across the world. But at the end of the day, we are still not very much represented. But as I said, it's positive because things are moving forward slowly but surely." And when we talk about some of these things with the leaky pipeline, what contributes to this problem? What are these forces that maybe are headwinds to this? "Well, if there was only one thing, it would be easy to tackle it. It's a number of things that come together. It comes from stereotypes, from gender bias, and from unconscious bias regarding what is a good profession for a young girl or for a young boy." "This changes the biases throughout your career. Whether when a child is born, the kind of toys they're going to be given, or then at school when the teachers are going to be thinking that girls are more for the arts and literature and the boys perhaps for physics. These are still very present today." "I even feel it already with my children. I've got two boys and two girls. And you see that the way that the teachers are guiding them to choose career paths and higher studies, it goes completely in that way." And this kind of dovetails into us talking a little bit more about these different stages of the pipeline. You just talked about education and sometimes in the early days maybe people are steered one way or another, maybe not even on purpose. What can be done at the education stage to sort of help fix these little holes in that pipe? "So I can give you an example of what we're doing right now in France. Because we have seen studies and empirical evidence has shown that the age where there's a lot of drop-out from girls' interest in STEM education is about 14 to 15 years old. And this is a very crucial age." "So what we've done are specific things like boot camps and workshops for girls between that age, but also for their parents and for the teachers. Because we have to train and we have to inspire those girls at that age, but also the parents. Because if they go home and they don't get the support from the parents, things don't work." "And if the teachers that are teaching the classes and guiding them also don't believe in that, things don't work. So we have to have a collective effort and collective work to guide those children to higher education." And what makes those programs work? Because there's been plenty of programs with girls in tech and we still haven't sort of bridged that gap. So for the most effective programs for girls in tech, what features do they have to have? "I think they are the ones that inspire the girls. There was a study done on different ways how you could inspire girls to get more results in having girls in education. and there were two different groups. One was they had speakers that were saying, 'Oh, we need more girls because girls are underrepresented, etc.'" "And the other group giving them role models that would inspire them on how these role models became successful and how they changed the world. And you can see that after the courses, the girls who attended the session which was an inspirational session, they were much more inspired to go to those higher education paths." "So you have to tackle the problem, I think, by inspiring the women to go to that thing. Saying okay, how they can change the world. Tech for good, for example. Those are the things that generally talk more to the girls." What sort of can go sideways? What can go wrong at early career that we might start losing women at even maybe their first couple years in their field? What goes wrong and how can we fix it? "It's I think it's the environment, right? When you are looking at an environment that is going to be welcoming for women, you can feel it right away. And this you can see by the rate of the drop-out of the women going out of the field, which is much higher than the men leaving the field." "Also, if it comes to an age where women maybe they want to found families, the structure within the company is going to be something that's going to push them to come back into the workforce. And also mentoring. We say 'Yes, yes,' you know, we need mentoring, we need women to be visible. I think visibility is something so important. Be seen, be heard, and make sure that the doors are also opened." And if I'm a leader listening to this and I want to do more, what's a question they can ask themselves about their own workplace or their teams to kind of help bridge any gaps that they might not even realize are there? "First of all, they have to look at their team, look at them around. Is it gender equal? Are there women, are there men? And even if they are, what are they bringing to the table? Sometimes women they feel that they don't belong or they're not being heard. Ask them questions." "When you are in a meeting, make sure everyone's views are being seen, whether you're a woman or a man, but try and get the views from everybody. And if they don't dare to say, ask questions and make sure they're there." And is there something special that needs to happen at late career for those women to make sure that they can maximize their talents and skills to an organization? What can we make sure doesn't stand in their way? "So first we have to have more women on boards and in leadership positions. When you look today, it's only about 10% of women who are in those levels. So having more women is important because I think the direction and the culture of a company comes from the top down." "So we have to make sure that they are walking the talk. And so having women leaders at that level will make sure that they're going to be a role model for the other women in the company. And they're going to be making sure as well that there're going to be more women that are going to be trained and inspired to come up the ladder." LinkedIn had its recent Workforce Confidence Index, and it found that women acknowledge AI would impact their jobs, but also that AI would fuel the importance of soft skills. So soft skills don't go away because of the ascendance of AI. What are the soft skills that AI can't learn, in your opinion? "For sure, I think it's empathy. Empathy is one of the main skills that they cannot learn, and discernment as well." How can leaders make sure that they can keep their soft skills sharp? What would you suggest? "By having diverse teams for sure. Keeping the human in the center of any team, not the tech. Tech is important but I think the human side, the relationship side, this is what makes a team thrive or not thrive." "And this is the core of every single successful business is a team. Making sure that there's a diverse team and then they can really foster I think conversations, open dialogue, and questioning how the personnel, how do they feel, are they happy in there, how can they improve. And these are the things that can only come from the leadership." You mentioned that maybe women aren't as well represented in AI as men, but they might be better prepared for a world that is giving more emphasis on soft skills. Is that correct? What kind of advantage would women have regarding soft skills as AI grows? "I think women do have an advantage with the soft skills, about empathy, because they nurture. This has been proven in many, many studies showing how women have this emotional side that has empathy in it. And so I think in that sense, yes." "But if you look at AI itself, you know, AI is just prediction. There's no ghost behind the machine, it's just a formula of prediction. And so it's only going to be coming out from the questions; it's a series of predictions that's going to be coming together." "At the end of the day, the results they give are for the human to decide whether that answer is ethical, if it really represents society, if it represents the culture and the values of the company. And this discernment and decision making can be done only by humans, right? They take the decision. And I think women are good in making these decisions because they have empathy." You are the founder of Women in Tech Global. What is that and what problem does it solve? "So Women in Tech Global, we are an organization with headquarters in Paris, but we are present in over 60 countries around the world with teams and we try to empower women in many different ways. We have four pillars: education, business, digital inclusion and advocacy." "We have a talent hub. We try to prepare women from the classroom all the way to the boardroom to come into the STEM fields." And you guys launched this thing called the Osaka Protocol just this year. What is that and what impact is it helping to make possible? "So the Osaka Protocol is a multilateral framework where we bring in different actors to the table where they are committing to help bridge this gender equality gap that exists in STEM fields. But in a very concrete way. It's not just pledging and saying, 'Yes, we want to have more women in education.'" "They have to say how they're going to be doing it, right? So they pledge, 'We are going to be training 100,000 women or 200,000 women,' but collectively we're going to be impacting 100 million women by 2030. But I think it will raise more awareness on how important it is to have more women there." "Because not only it's important for the business, but also for women to be financially independent and for the companies themselves to be able to perform better. I think 100 million is something that can start causing systemic change, and I would like to see us moving from a 28% representation of women in the workforce all the way up to 45%." What sort of prepared you to run Women in Tech Global? In your background that sort of poised you to make you ready for this? "I don't think we're ever really ready for it. I was an entrepreneur before. So I think being an entrepreneur, knowing that you have to go over walls, open doors, climb windows, and go over the roof because it's hard—it's very hard—is something that can really help you." "And it also comes from a background, you know, I grew up in South Africa in Johannesburg where it was still apartheid, where we had to fight different kinds of biases, of discrimination. There was racial discrimination, but there was also a lot of gender discrimination back then." "And so this is how I grew up. And so having to fight those stereotypes, you know, at school, at sport, it really forged a character to not become a victim in any situation and to fight for the rights." You mentioned walls, hitting walls. What's a wall that you hit that was really shaping for you? Where you really learned a lesson from it and it changed maybe how you solve problems or how you make decisions? "I've had many walls; every day there is a wall. But maybe like the very first one was when I founded Women in Tech. I thought this was such an important organization and such an important movement because we are there to empower women, that we needed so much help." "So I thought I was going to be getting help from many partners, from many sponsors, from many people. When I organized my first event, I thought I would have partners and sponsors behind me and nothing. Not one Euro. So I had to bring out, you know, make up this very big awards event all by myself." "Self-funding, selling t-shirts with children... so it was it was a difficult but, you know, what doesn't kill you makes you stronger. Everything is a small fight, but it pays off at the end." Is there an advice that you would give to yourself looking back? Tell yourself anything, what would you tell her? "Use my voice much earlier. I found my voice at a later stage and, you know, it was a journey to find that. I don't regret anything in my life. The only thing I do regret is when I didn't say things I should have said at the right time." "Don't think about it unless like you're in real anger, but you have to take the opportunity. Tomorrow you might not have it. So take it when you have it and say it." What do entrepreneurs need right now? "If you look at the global statistics of global VC funding last year, so 2024, the most recent, only 2.6% of global funding went to all-women teams. 2%. That's ridiculous. Yes, it's good to have accelerators. Yes, it's good to have mentoring, to have training, but they need funding." It's been an ongoing issue, women and funding, they've gotten a fraction of it, to the point that some people may have tuned this out. It doesn't seem new and fresh. How can we get people to pay attention? "I think the more that the women are going to be changing the world, if I may say, whether they're making unicorns but also advancing in science. Because there're more and more women coming to science and tackling global problems. These are the ways that they're going to be really changing the narrative." "Knowing that women are behind innovations, I think giving visibility to women and making sure that their names are out there, that we put the spotlight on them. Not only on the women, but also on the inventions and the innovations that they do. Because we don't have to reinvent the wheel." "I think putting them forward will inspire and also give tribute to the remarkable work that's being done." You're a self-taught coder. What have you been teaching yourself now? What's the latest thing you've been learning? "So I started a doctoral degree so right now I'm a doctoral candidate and I'm learning to really learn. I'm doing research. So this is something that's really opening my mind in a great way. I think it's one of the best things I've done. Yeah." "I'm doing research and I'm working right now on tech diplomacy and its impact, and how it can impact and influence gender equality and women empowerment." Is there a fact that has been really surprising to you from what you've learned in your research so far? "Yes. So I did a research, it was a quantitative research on gender bias in AI. We talk about a lot about it but then to find the data and to find, you know, real proof, it was quite something. So we found there was gender bias in large language models." "And depending on the language model in the AI platforms—I'm not going to cite them, but we put together like five different ones—and depending on the one, they were not always the same bias. So there are some platforms they're more biased than others. And there are languages that are more biased than others." "So you have neutral English that is a neutral language, so you find less bias. However, if you go to Latin languages like Portuguese or French or Spanish, it is higher. Also languages like German which is not a Latin language but is a gendered language, like Russian and Arabic, you also find it." "So depending on the country, depending on the databases that they get, and depending on the platform you use, you have more or less gender bias." People will be maybe managing AI agents as opposed to maybe people. Maybe even managing multi-agent systems. What skills will people have to develop so that they can better manage either AI agents or these multi-AI agent systems? "So obviously you have to learn how to prompt, but you have to learn how to define your question in a way that can be easily comprehended. But not only prompting. Yes, you have to ask the questions, but most importantly, you have to verify the answer." "Don't take anything that's just given to you as if it was the truth. Go check the answer and check the data where the answer was got. I know it's a lot of work, but honestly it's so important. Because for example, even for my research work, I would say, 'Okay, give me the top academic papers on this field.'" "They would give me the top ones. I said, 'Give me the reference.' They gave me the reference. But if you click on it and you go look at the data, it doesn't exist. It was false. So you have to check every single step until you make sure that the answer is vital." "This is vital for us to have the right information. You have to check. So prompt right and then check, check and recheck. Misinformation can lead to drastic consequences. Because if you are making decisions with wrong information, then things can get bad." Is there anything that you're worried about? You think, "Gosh, this is something people really need to focus on." In your mind, if they could prioritize one big gnarly problem to be solving, what is that? "Honestly, what's worrying right now is all the backlash that's done on gender equality, on diversity. For me that's something very worrying. Every time you go like one step forward, you go two steps back." "But at the same time, I think it's important to see the true face of people and companies and to know what the values are. And I think that today integrity is one of the hardest things to find. And when you find it, whether it's in a person, a structure, an organization, this is something very valuable." "And this is the place where you can count on and you have to invest and you have to be able to get the best people there because those are the ones that are worth it and they can make a difference." What's a tactical way that a leader can still make sure that they are being inclusive and that they are kind of keeping their eye on these equality goals? "It goes all the way from the hiring procedure that they've got in the companies to the environment that they are creating in the company. I think if it's in the core, if it's in the DNA of an organization to be open, to be diverse, to be welcoming to people of all genders and natures and backgrounds, this brings richness, right?" "So I think it shows the open-mindedness and the intelligence of the leader, of the company, and also the integrity they have." Is there something that you do now that would not have occurred to you at the start of your career? "I'm very data-oriented right now. So I check the data. So even when I'm reading a paper and they say they mention like a number, I say 'Yeah, they say this, but what is it behind?' And so many times even like in big papers, you go check the source, it doesn't say the same thing that they are meaning." "So I try to find the source of the data and interpret it myself. and then you see that sometimes our data is dirty. So you can make it say so many different things. Finding the core and then making your own decision about it, you know, like your own thoughts, your own reflection, I think that's something super important." Is there a book that you recommend? "This book is called Who Not How. And I think it's a wonderful book because it shows how you don't have to know how to do everything. Things can be done by surrounding yourself with the right people." And how would someone change after they read that? "They change because they look for people that can help you in every single stage of your life to facilitate your life and to make you be able to perform better and to do more." Is there one theme that you think is going to define the next 12 months in technology? "Sovereignty. The instrumentalization of AI and of nations. There's this Cold War right now on AI. And I think that the balance of digital sovereignty and of collective work and of innovation is going to be central." "It's not only for the main superpower that we have like East and West, but also bringing into consideration the global south that I think is going to be coming up and we have to make sure that we hear voices. There's going to be this paradigm shift in power and influence." And for listeners who maybe are not familiar, can you define digital sovereignty for us? "Digital sovereignty is a state-nation ownership of the data of the rights that encompass all the digital frameworks, for example, AI, and how that data in the nation state is being owned, is being used and can be exchanged." And if we get digital sovereignty right, what does the world look like? "I like to see this... it's almost when globalization works right. If you look at digital sovereignty for in an ethical way, we're making sure that we are putting the human and the ethical side at the center and it's being respected by all the countries, the nation states." "I think we're talking about peace and about security and about a world that's more stable that's going to be able to work together in many different ways. If we were able to bring that together, I think peace is possible and many things are possible." Is there a piece of advice that you've always been grateful for? "Yes, there's this lady said to me, when I told her my dream about Women in Tech and about this world where there's going to be more gender parity. She said to me, 'Once you find your dream, you have to be able to articulate it and explain it in a very simple way so that people understand what it is.'" "'And you have to inspire them. This way they will be able to help you and to achieve things in a much faster way.'" That was Ayumi Moore Aoki. Thanks so much to her. And thanks so much to you for listening. To read our recent Gender Parity in the Intelligent Age report, go to the show notes of this episode. We'll have that and our recent edition of the Global Gender Gap report. And to listen to more podcasts, including my colleague's podcast, Radio Davos, go to wef.ch/podcasts. That's it for now. I'm Linda Lacina from the World Economic Forum. Have a great day.

Robert Smith: AI, Enterprise Software and the Future of Investing

Creative Planning(42.0K) ·44:22 ·983 views · 2026-02-24
Robert Smith (Founder, Chairman & CEO, Vista Equity Partners) Peter Mallouk (President & CEO, Creative Planning)
Robert Smith argues that the next frontier of value creation is the "Agnetic" enterprise, where AI agents integrated into proprietary workflows allow software companies to capture trillions in service-sector revenue.
TL;DR Robert Smith argues that the next frontier of value creation is the "Agnetic" enterprise, where AI agents integrated into proprietary workflows allow software companies to capture trillions in service-sector revenue. The key to maintaining a competitive moat is bringing AI...

TL;DR

Robert Smith argues that the next frontier of value creation is the "Agnetic" enterprise, where AI agents integrated into proprietary workflows allow software companies to capture trillions in service-sector revenue. The key to maintaining a competitive moat is bringing AI models to proprietary data, rather than "leeching" enterprise value by feeding data into foundational public models.

The Evolution to "Agnetic" Enterprise Software

  • Deterministic vs. Probabilistic Outcomes: While Large Language Models (LLMs) are "probabilistic" (mostly right), enterprise software must remain "deterministic" (exactly right).
      • Businesses cannot rely on 93% accuracy for wire transfers or insurance underwriting; they require repeatable, reliable systems.
  • TAM Expansion: Smith predicts a $1 trillion increase in the Total Addressable Market (TAM) as AI enables software to "eat" the services industry.
      • Case Study: One Vista portfolio company replaced a $5 million outsourced services contract with an automated "Agnetic" solution. This shifted the revenue from a $250,000 software seat license to a multi-million dollar contract, representing a 10x expansion.
  • The Rule of 70: In the AI era, the traditional "Rule of 40" (growth rate + EBITDA margin) is evolving toward a "Rule of 70" for top-tier Agnetic companies that achieve extreme efficiency and scale.

Data Sovereignty and the AI Moat

  • Model-to-Data Strategy: Smith warns against "leeching" enterprise value into foundational models.
      • Strategic Imperative: Organizations must bring the AI model to their data, not their data to the model.
      • Data Scarcity: Less than 1% of the world's enterprise data is currently included in foundational LLM training sets.
  • Sovereignty of Workflows: The "incumbent advantage" lies in possessing the specific workflows and historical data of an industry (e.g., specific state regulations for insurance). AI-native startups often lack the context and deterministic guardrails to displace these incumbents easily.

Investment Philosophy: The Engineering Mindset

  • Designed Experiments: Vista manages approximately 90 portfolio companies as a "designed experiment." They test new technologies (like early access to Anthropic or OpenAI) in a few firms and then diffuse successful patterns across the entire portfolio.
  • Informed Intuition: Smith defines his "gut feeling" as an intuition informed by repeated fact patterns across a massive data set of software performance.
  • Private Market Dominance: 96% of enterprise software companies are private. Smith notes that being private allows for the "nimbleness" required to survive the "messy middle" of technological transitions, such as the current shift from SaaS to Agnetic systems.

The Future of Labor and Education

  • AI-Assisted Production: Vista has seen an 80% increase in productivity regarding code generation by using AI tools.
      • Advice for Students: Smith previously advised students to learn to code; he now advises them to learn how to use AI to write code.
  • Job Displacement: The creative destruction of AI will likely see the 20% of workers who master AI tools replacing the 80% who do not.
      • Automation of Mundane Tasks: Low-level tasks and high-volume monitoring are the most vulnerable. However, AI will also solve labor shortages in sectors like local government, where millions of workers are reaching retirement age.

Philanthropy and Personal Resilience

  • The Morehouse Gift: Smith’s decision to pay off student debt for the Morehouse Class of 2019 was a strategic "bet" on individuals who had already bet on themselves. He also covered "Parent PLUS" loans to alleviate the burden on the students' support systems.
  • Keys to Success: Smith identifies "Optimism and Intention" as the two most critical traits for those feeling "locked out" of the current economy.
      • The Process: Intentions must be actualized in three stages: first through the mind, second through words, and third through consistent action.
"Enterprise software is the most productive tool that's been introduced in our business economy over the last 50 years. And likely will be for the next 50." — Robert Smith
"Bring the model to the data, not your data to the model... If you leech your data into those foundational models, you have now diffused a lot of enterprise value into other organizations." — Robert Smith
"What AI enables enterprise software to do is to eat services... We go from $250,000 [in revenue] to two and a half million dollars plus. 10x expansion of TAM because the AI enabled our enterprise software to eat the services." — Robert Smith
"Twenty percent of you who use AI will actually replace the other 80 [percent]." — Robert Smith (referencing a law firm CEO)
On our last episode of Icons and Ideas, I interviewed Tony Robbins. Tony Robbins had written a book where he got to interview 50 or more people in the world of finance. In the middle of that interview, you might recall, I asked him who was the person that impressed him the most of all the people he'd interviewed for that book and the 50 people he interviewed from the first book. Now, mind you, you had Warren Buffett, Bogle... the list is endless of superstars. And he said, "Do you know Robert Smith?" And I said, "You got to be kidding me, because I do know Robert Smith and he's the next person I'm interviewing for Icons and Ideas." And that's probably the best intro I can give to the founder and president and CEO and visionary of Vista. Perfect timing to have you on this podcast. Peter, it is good to be here. Just a funny story about that, you know, I get a chance to meet with wonderful people and exciting people around the world. I was talking to my wife and I said, "Oh, you know, Tony Robbins," and that was the only one she got excited about. It's the one my kids listen to, so it's mutual admiration. Thanks for inviting me on the program. I am excited to be here. Well, so there are some of our viewers who are more generalists; this isn't the world that they live in. And I've been telling our teams for years: the key is bring the model to the data, not your data to the model. Okay? Because in essence, enterprise data—less than 1% of enterprise data actually exists in these foundational models and what they've been trained on. And so if you have data in your infrastructure, in your company, in the industry, bringing the models and utilizing this technology to enable you to use that more effectively is going to create massive enterprise value. If you leach your data into those foundational models, you have now diffused a lot of enterprise value into other organizations. And so that's an important construct. And Satya was saying, "This is something not a lot of people are talking about," but it was one of the most important things we have to think about now that these models have become much more generally available in terms of how enterprises use them. Look, you and I as consumers, we've already leeched our data. Right? Because we consume things in ways that others have access to our preferences and the dynamics of how we want to engage with people or companies, etc. But in the enterprise, that isn't necessarily the case. Like I said, IBM put out a report that less than 1% of this enterprise data is actually part of these foundational models. This is a key and pivotal point for enterprises to maintain their viability going forward: it is to bring, again, these models to their data, not their data to the models. We were talking a lot about business-to-business software and subscription-based services. Can you give like an example of any company, whether it's in your portfolio or not, just so our listeners that aren't into this as much have a sense of what we are even talking about? Sure. B2B software, we call enterprise. These are business systems. And so you take insurance, for instance. If you are an insurance company, pick it, you know, there's ways that they underwrite a property and casualty insurance claim, or the ways they underwrite a life insurance policy, or the ways they manage a claim for any of these things. It's the software that governs the workflows around, you know, you live in Palo Alto, California, you have a house of this size, and they need to underwrite a fire policy or whatever it might be. There's certain rules and regulations, which may be federal and state in many cases, that live within the policy management systems. There's all of course who you are and your name and all that kind of stuff, but there's the ways that they have to underwrite so that when you are getting an insurance policy and your neighbor is getting one, they have to be underwritten the same as opposed to probabilistically different. And like I always like to say, that's why these LLM models, if you think about them, the way that they were built on this whole transformer technology is they are probabilistic, not deterministic. Enterprise software gives you deterministic outcomes. You have a repeatability to those outcomes as opposed to it being mostly right. I'm sure you'd be upset if your wire transfers for your paycheck were mostly right. You want it to be exactly right. It's that dynamic, so you take that and start to apply it across the infrastructure of business. Businesses don't want to be mostly right. They need deterministic outcomes. And so how you build and take probabilistic efficiencies into deterministic outcomes is part of what leads to what I call the Vista Agnetic Factory. So this was such a hot part—obviously tech companies were hot, and then this kind of subscription-based enterprise software was like the hottest. Because I think, speaking from my point of view, but—investors like this recurring revenue and built-in price adjustments. And it goes on forever and a big moat around it. You might be the number one person in the world to ask this question to right now. I mean, what do you think is happening? Yeah, a lot of it is people aren't informed as to what is actually occurring in the world of agnetic enterprise software. Look, everyone paints things with a broad brush. "Oh gee, how did this all come about?" You know, you had one of the big LLM providers issue a new code product that says, "Oh, I can now enable legal analysis and systems." And as a result of that, everyone who had enterprise software in and around kind of the Westlaws and the LexisNexis—that kind of spreads. And it's like, "Oh gee, this is going to eliminate the need for those businesses." Well, on the one hand, what you have to realize is the bulk of the information that is in the legal constructs, like an NDA and those sorts of things—that's already out there. It's already part of these learning management tools. There isn't anything necessarily proprietary about NDAs and NDA constructs and preparing a brief, etc. This is a tool that is a productivity tool that, in some respects, doesn't have the proprietary workflows and data sets that an enterprise software system has for underwriting an insurance claim. But it gets painted all with the same broad brush. This is likely going to be larger, because the economic impact for those that become agnetic is actually higher. Let me give you an example. If you look now, what shook people is this ARR dynamic, terminal value. "Oh, I have a customer who now pays me money to use this, a seat, a license. And they're going to use it forever." And so I'm going to underwrite my investment based on it being—They're never going to leave. There's a lock-in. The cost to switch is too high, it's such a hassle. Right, right. All that. And a lot of that continues to be very true. Here's the difference. If I am using one of our agnetic systems—agnetic meaning an agent operates within these workflows—the economic value of that utilization is orders of magnitude greater than the seat license. But there haven't been enough proof points in the marketplace for people to see that. And actually, there's reports—Bain just came out with a report, BCG came out with a report—it's like the TAM, total addressable market, in the BCG report, $1 trillion of increase to application providers who are going to deliver this to the enterprise. The question people are asking is: well, will AI-native companies have access to this more so than what we'll call those who already are participants in the industry? That's really going to be a function, Peter, of I call it the sovereignty and dominion of the workflows and data sets. Because the AI-native companies don't have necessarily the context. They don't have the data. They don't have the policy management information that's been built up within the workflows within companies. You're not going to take an agent and just drop it into an environment and get a series of probabilistic outcomes. Take a 93% times 93% times 93% as it goes through a workflow—that's not going to give you a deterministic outcome. So, those who actually are in possession of, I call it, the sovereignty and dominion of the workflows and data sets, have the greatest advantage. But you have to take advantage of that by innovating AI enablement, agnetic enablement of what is your incumbent advantage in that environment. So I understand that, so that in some systems it definitely feels a lot less vulnerable hearing you talk about this. So I like to say enterprise software—again, business-to-business software—will end up in one of three categories. Great question. Starting at the high level, often there's a conflation of software versus enterprise software and consumer software. Two different things.
