Inside Dan Sundheim's Bets on Anthropic, OpenAI, and SpaceX

Invest Like The Best(40.0K) ·86:51 ·13.1K views · 2026-02-24
Dan Sundheim (Founder and CIO, D1 Capital Partners) Patrick O'Shaughnessy (Host, Invest Like The Best)
Dan Sundheim argues that the AI revolution is fundamentally shifting market structures, specifically challenging the long-term dominance of cloud hyperscalers while creating a new "fixed-asset" business model for LLMs that blends the characteristics of Netflix and Spotify.
TL;DR Dan Sundheim argues that the AI revolution is fundamentally shifting market structures, specifically challenging the long-term dominance of cloud hyperscalers while creating a new "fixed-asset" business model for LLMs that blends the characteristics of Netflix and Spotify....

TL;DR

Dan Sundheim argues that the AI revolution is fundamentally shifting market structures, specifically challenging the long-term dominance of cloud hyperscalers while creating a new "fixed-asset" business model for LLMs that blends the characteristics of Netflix and Spotify.

The Convergence of Public and Private Markets

  • Synergy through Innovation: Sundheim notes that while private and public market analysis used to overlap only 25% of the time, the rapid rise of AI has made private market insights essential for public market positioning.
  • Competitive Dynamics:
      • Public Markets: Characterized by hyper-competition where participants are often "playing different sports" due to varying time horizons and irrational short-term pressures.
      • Private Markets: Less competitive in terms of sheer numbers, but highly competitive regarding access. Success depends on being an investor that the best companies *want* on their cap table.
  • Founder Quality: Sundheim draws a direct parallel between Dario Amodei (Anthropic) and Jeff Bezos, citing "clarity of thought" and the ability to communicate a long-term vision in written form as the primary indicators of a generational company.

The LLM Business Model: Netflix meets Spotify

  • The "Fixed-Asset" Framework: Sundheim describes LLMs as a hybrid of two distinct digital models:
      • The Netflix Element: These companies spend massive capital upfront to "train" the model (similar to content production). Once trained, the incremental cost to sell the model is negligible, leading to high margins if the scale is sufficient.
      • The Spotify Element: While the underlying "music" (or model output) may eventually become a commodity, stickiness is created through personalization. The more a model knows about a user's history and preferences, the higher the switching costs.
  • Focus vs. Expansion: He highlights a strategic divergence between OpenAI and Anthropic.
      • OpenAI: Pursuing an "everything" strategy (robotics, hardware, consumer, enterprise). Sundheim notes this is historically difficult to pull off but possible with the world's best talent.
      • Anthropic: Focusing almost exclusively on enterprise and coding, which has led to a perception of them "winning" the enterprise lane.
  • The Case for Advertising: Sundheim reveals he pushed OpenAI to adopt an ad model early, arguing that even the most "ad-allergic" companies (like Netflix) eventually succumb to the economic necessity of ads to compete.

Risks to Hyperscalers and Traditional Software

  • Hyperscaler Vulnerability: Sundheim holds a contrarian view that AWS, Azure, and GCP may become worse businesses over the next decade.
      • Financing Mechanisms: He believes LLMs currently use hyperscalers as "financing mechanisms" for compute. Once LLMs generate significant free cash flow, they are likely to insource their own data centers.
      • GPU vs. CPU: LLMs are often better at running GPU clusters and inference than traditional cloud providers, who are optimized for CPU workloads.
  • The "Software Short": AI is creating the first real "short" opportunities in the software sector.
      • Vibe Coding: While simple apps can now be generated instantly, "Systems of Record" (like ERPs or CRMs) are safer in the near term because enterprises are unlikely to trust mission-critical infrastructure to "vibe coding."
      • Adaptation Requirement: Software companies must evolve like Walmart did during the e-commerce rise—investing heavily and accepting margin pressure to remain relevant.

Resilience and Market Structure

  • The GameStop Era: Sundheim reflects on the 2021 short squeeze as a period of extreme professional adversity.
      • Internal Stability: To maintain morale during the drawdown, he told his team that January’s losses would be excluded from their compensation calculations to focus on the future.
      • Reputation Recovery: He emphasizes that in a crisis, it is impossible to disprove a negative narrative in the short term; reputation is rebuilt slowly and methodically over years, not months.
  • Economic Optimism: Despite geopolitical risks, Sundheim is "maximum bullish" on economic growth. He views AI as the ultimate productivity tool that allows for growth alongside disinflation—the "nirvana" for market returns.

Notable Quotes

"I place a lot of weight on clarity of thought and the ability to communicate as a CEO... Dario [Amodei] just did that better than almost any CEO I’ve seen since Bezos." — Dan Sundheim
"I think that the hyperscalers are a worse business model going forward... If you roll out 10 years from now, I think the majority of the workloads will probably be AI, and it will make economic sense [for LLMs] to take it in-house." — Dan Sundheim
"We are no longer going to be the most intelligent beings on the planet. And so what are the implications of that? I’m not really sure." — Dan Sundheim
"It’s better to go through life being an optimist and be proved wrong than a pessimist and be proved right." — Dan Sundheim (referencing Elon Musk)
My guest today is Dan Sundheim, the founder and CIO of D1 Capital Partners. Anyone that loves investing and markets has surely heard Dan's name before. I think you'll very quickly realize that he is one of the most passionate investors operating in the world today. That D1 operates across markets, both across sectors in public markets and having invested in some of the most valuable and important private market companies like SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic, is just a testament to how universal Dan's curiosity is about companies and markets. I find Dan to be one of the quickest, smartest thinkers about just about any company. When we recorded this conversation in his office in Miami, I spent the next hour talking to him about name after name after name, and I could not believe the deep clarity and understanding that he had about a small private markets company, then a big public markets company in technology, then another public company that was an industrial. He could move all over the map, and it's incredibly clear that he spends all of his waking hours studying markets and studying companies. The takeaway for me from this conversation is how good you can be when you are as passionate about what you do as Dan is about investing. Please enjoy this great and wide-ranging conversation on all things markets with Dan Sundheim. I want to spend a bunch of time talking about public versus private. You do both. You started investing in privates more than 10 years ago. You were kind of one of the pioneers of this. You've got some amazing huge private positions, SpaceX and lots of others. Draw the contrast today in 2024 of the difference in how the two markets feel. I'm curious about a lot of things here, like how you think about valuation differences, what one tells you about the other, you know, the business of privates versus a public equity hedge fund. I want to go into kind of all of it, but at a high level, what is your feeling on the difference between the two markets? It changes over time, so it depends where you are in a cycle. I'd say right now I think that there's a lot of interesting opportunities in late-stage privates. It's a moment in time. There's some companies that you've never seen—these are some of the largest companies in the world by market cap that are private right now. And not only are they large and private, they are innovating in a way that's going to change the world, right? So this moment is particularly interesting. I think that in general private markets are less competitive. There's obviously the core skill set of analyzing businesses that creates the majority of value, but there's other aspects of it too. Oftentimes there's no disagreement among private investors that a certain company is excellent, but that company has to want you to be an investor in the company. So it's competitive from the standpoint of being able to create a situation where you can invest in the best companies, but in terms of just pure how difficult is it to generate returns by assessing companies, I'd say the public markets are the most competitive in the world. Even though they are less efficient than they were before, it's still a situation where you have more people in more places looking at information in companies. Whereas on the private side, just by definition, you have fewer people looking at every situation and less capital. One difference that equalizes it a bit is that you don't have this dynamic on the private side of people doing things that are economically irrational because they're focused on the short term or their business model is not consistent with investing based on long-term intrinsic value, whereas you have that in the public markets. In the private market, every time we're looking at a business, everybody's doing the same thing. We can talk to other firms that are investing in the same company; their research may be slightly different than ours, but it is all trying to get at the same answer. That's very different than the public market. So there's fewer people competing, but they're all doing the same thing, whereas the public market's just tons of people competing, but they're all playing a different sport. If you think about the key companies in your private portfolio today—Anthropic, OpenAI, companies like SpaceX or Ramp, etc.—what does that group teach you? What do you think you see coming that maybe the public markets don't fully appreciate yet, that don't have that same exposure to these great private businesses? As long as I've been doing private and public investing, at some points in time, there's synergy. But I'd say if you go back to when we founded the firm, 25% of the time we looked at a private company, there was some synergy with what we were doing on the public side. Now, because of AI and because there's so much innovation happening in the private markets, the synergies are just greater than I've ever seen before. I think if you're going to take a view on public companies that are deeply impacted by AI, which eventually will be almost every public company, you should have an opinion on where the technology is now, where the technology is going, and what the implications of it are. Investing in those companies, I think, gives you that perspective in a way that I've never seen greater synergy. When you first were considering your initial investments in OpenAI and Anthropic, did you pattern match their businesses or their business models on anything that you had seen historically? Did they remind you of anything? They were very different in that when we first invested in OpenAI, I wouldn't say it was contrarian at all. To some extent, we invested originally at the 25 billion dollar round, so I don't think people were entirely sold on LLMs as a business model, but if you want to invest in LLMs as a business model, OpenAI was kind of the one. Whether you invested in LLMs or didn't invest in LLMs was debated quite a bit. I think there was a lot of uncertainty about the ultimate business model of these companies, so that was what we had to figure out. Anthropic was a different situation. When we first invested in Anthropic, a number of people that I spoke to who I think are very smart drew the analogy of Uber versus Lyft. Why are you going to invest in the second player? In most industries, investing in the second player is not the path to glory. The way I viewed it was it was incredibly difficult at that stage to say who was going to be first and who was going to be second. And the pattern recognition, to answer your question, for me on Anthropic was just reading Dario's essays and listening to him on podcasts. When I look back on my career and look back at the companies we missed, like Amazon in the early days, and I think, "What could I have seen?" If you look at their income statement, you would just have seen a sea of red; you wouldn't have seen anything. The only telltale sign was like reading Jeff Bezos's 1997 shareholder letter, which showed the clarity of thought and his understanding of what he wanted to achieve. How to create value for shareholders was greater than almost any public CEO I dealt with. If I had read that and almost ignored everything else, it would have been a really important sign and very profitable. Dario struck me like that. It wasn't that the models at that point were so differentiated. I think they were considered to be one of probably maybe at that point five, six, or seven players that could ultimately be important. There was still a lot of debate around LLMs as a business model, but I felt like he was incredibly skilled and extremely focused. I place a lot of weight, right or wrong, on clarity of thought and the ability to communicate as a CEO—what you want to achieve and how you're going to achieve it. Especially in written form, because taking the time to write something down, you actually really have to go through everything you plan to do and express it in a way that makes sense to everybody else. And Dario just did that better than almost any CEO I've seen since Bezos. As your business scales up, everything gets more complex, especially your compliance and security needs. With so many tools offering bandaids and patches, it's unfortunately far too easy for something to slip through the cracks. Fortunately, Vanta is a powerful tool designed to simplify and automate your security work and deliver a single source of truth for compliance and risk. There's a reason that Ramp, Cursor, and Snowflake all use Vanta. It frees them up to focus on building amazing differentiated products, knowing that compliance and security are under control. Learn more at vanta.com/invest. I know firsthand how complex the tech stack is for asset managers, and seemingly every new tool and data source makes the problem even worse, adding more complexity, more headcount, and more risk. Ridgeline offers a better way forward: one unified platform that automates away all that complexity across portfolio accounting, reconciliation, reporting, trading, compliance, and more, all at scale. Ridgeline is revolutionizing investment management, helping ambitious firms scale faster, operate smarter, and stay ahead of the curve. See what Ridgeline can unlock for your firm. Schedule a demo at ridgelineapps.com. How would you frame the debate today about LLMs as a business model, now that we know a bit more? Back then, it was like, "Are these businesses going to ever generate an economic return?" I think one analogy was that AI will be huge, but so was air travel, and airlines were not a good business. There's just nothing differentiating about one airline from the other, and so therefore returns go down to the cost of capital. Obviously we took a different view, but that was like a 65-35 or 70-30 degree of confidence in that at that point. It was more about the skew; if things played out like we thought and the business models were actually moated, it would be huge. I think at this point we're in a different place in terms of the debate that's important. If you want to look through a positive lens, businesses have taken slightly different lanes and have excelled at different things within AI. OpenAI has been great at consumer and has had good traction in enterprise. Anthropic has been incredibly successful at coding. There was a thesis when we first invested that APIs, or the business of having other software companies plug into your model, would be commoditized because they could just one day use your model and one day use another. It was thought to be a race to the bottom. I think that debate is more or less irrelevant because you've just seen with Claude Code and even OpenAI's API business that these are durable businesses. Yes, can you switch? You can, the same way you could switch AWS or Azure, but it's not worth it for a lot of businesses to do it, and there's sufficient differentiation among the models. If you look at the underlying margins of these companies, they are not the margins that you see in a commoditized industry. The gross margins are quite high. The competitive landscape, I think, is not heavily debated. At this point you probably have four or five LLMs that will be relevant in the long term. I don't see that changing. Not that there's not sufficient talent out there, it's just that the capital required to get into this business is too great and these companies are too big at this point. Then you kind of get the snowball effect: the more capital you have, the more compute you get, and you get better researchers. I think it would be very difficult for new players. So the competitive landscape's not really in question. I don't think anyone would say that these business models are commoditized. I think the real debate is that these are extremely capital intensive businesses, capital intensive to a degree that we've never seen before in the history of business. The question is you're spending a ton of capital and the ultimate return on that capital is unknown. It's not like a normal business that builds a factory and kind of knows what they're going to sell. You are spending tons of capital to train a model, and the question is do the scaling laws work such that the returns on that capital continue to be attractive? If they do, it means that you will be able to attract more capital and build better models. Or are you going to get to a point where everyone looks back and says we raised too much money, we spent too much training models, and we didn't get the economic return? Equally likely if not more likely, people would say ultimately you will get the economic return, but it just happened slower than you would have thought. Enterprise adoption just didn't take off as quickly as you thought, and therefore the problem is when you are this capital intensive, it introduces financial leverage and operating leverage to a degree you don't see in normal businesses. You don't have the luxury of two or three years of things going slower than you otherwise would expect. The scaling laws, the returns on capital, and the speed at which these tools and AI is adopted throughout the economy are the questions. If you think about the competition—not just current competition but potential future competition—is there anything that Netflix could teach us? That's another business where a crazy amount of capital was spent to build an asset and then it gets amortized over a bigger and bigger user base. That's turned out to be a great stock and one that I know you've owned a lot. Is there any analogy between those two that's interesting to you? When I was speaking to the executives at the LLMs, I framed it this way: I think your business is some kind of combination between Netflix and Spotify. Netflix in that unlike other tech companies, you are spending a ton of money upfront to train these models. Once these models are trained, you go sell them at extremely high incremental margins. You don't know what the revenues are going to be from that fixed asset that you've built, but to the extent that you've built that asset, you want to sell it as much as possible so that you can get the cash flows to build the next model and so on and so forth. That's very similar to Netflix in that they invested in content. When you're an early mover in this kind of fixed asset business, you invest heavily, you get the capital to invest heavily, you get the revenues, and you spread it out over an increasing number of people. You invest more in that fixed asset and that just kind of has a flywheel effect. Eventually you get to the point where it's almost impossible to compete because it's just a first-mover advantage. If you were to say what is an important difference of Netflix versus these models, it is that Netflix's content was differentiated. The models are more similar than they are different. At any given time, OpenAI may have a better model, or Anthropic may have a better model, but a lot of the expertise and the innovation gets disseminated pretty quickly. So these models are not terribly different. And that's where the Spotify analogy comes in. I think if you're Google or you are OpenAI, the differentiating factor will not necessarily be that Google gives you a better answer. If we were just to query Gemini or ChatGPT on something, I don't think we would say definitively that one will give you a better answer over time. However, the personalization matters. The first-mover advantage is like the more that these models know about you, how you live your life, your health, all the things that are important to you, you build up this history with it and it becomes very sticky. The music on Spotify is no different than Apple Music or Amazon Music, right? It's theoretically a pure commodity. What makes Spotify have pricing power? What makes it differentiated? Why would people be incredibly upset if you said they had to stop using Spotify? It's because it's personalized. They've tailored their service to take a product which is a commodity and personalize it to the point where you're willing to pay a premium for it. If you were giving advice to the executives at these companies and telling them what to lean into and what to look out for over the next five years, what would you say? The scaling laws are so interesting in the sense that the models keep getting better, which probably means the revenue available could be the whole world. But the cost keeps going up by orders of magnitude—the Colossus 2 data center is like this unfathomably big thing with two gigawatts of power. What advice would you give them based on everything you've learned about these big massive businesses? The really interesting thing and challenging aspect of these businesses—the LLMs—is that the models they are building now and especially in the future can be applied to almost any aspect of the economy. You can take these models and make consumers' lives more efficient as personal assistants, you could solve physics problems, you could help with drug discovery, or you could make enterprises more efficient. The TAM is certainly not the problem. Focus is going to be a question mark. On the one hand, the more markets you go after with a fixed asset, the better, right? Because you're just spreading that cost over more markets and having more revenue, which then can be reinvested. The flip side of that is that I rarely have seen any company succeed trying to go after multiple end markets at the same time. You have an A-team, and that A-team is focused on one thing. Your culture as a company is oriented towards either consumer or enterprise. They just have different needs. Even Amazon, which you'd say is the example of a consumer company that got into enterprise, they got into it seven years later, even after they went public. So trying to do everything at once is tempting because if you're successful, you're effectively just amortizing that fixed asset over more revenue streams. At the same time, you risk not being the best at any one thing. That is the tradeoff. And I think that I'm not sure we have the final answer. Right now, the market has gone through periods where they thought Anthropic was Lyft and OpenAI was Uber, and now up until recently the sentiment on OpenAI was more negative. I think OpenAI is taking the strategy of "let's do everything." We are going to go after hardware, we're going to go after robotics, we're going to go after enterprise, consumer, and science. They've been very successful in a lot of ways, but that's hard. I'm sure there are companies I'm not thinking of, but I can't think of many examples where that's been successful. I understand the temptation to do it, and obviously the difference versus history is that the smartest people in the world are all going to work at these companies. So if anyone's going to pull it off, they will. Anthropic took a different approach and just said, "We are going to focus on enterprise." They tried consumer early on, but it became clear they didn't have traction, so then they just went all in on enterprise. They've had a lot of success with coding and enterprise. Because they've now taken a market-leading position, the general sentiment is that Anthropic is winning and they are now the Uber, if you want to use that analogy. I think this is going to go back and forth over time and people will probably get carried away in both directions, but I think those are the biggest differences. I would probably err on the side of focus, but I do understand the economic rationale for trying to do as many things as once. Early on, we invested in OpenAI—this was probably a year and a half ago—I said to them, "You have to do ads." I understand—I've seen it so many times—people in Silicon Valley are often allergic to the idea of ads. "I had this amazing pure technology product and you want me to taint it with ads." But you see companies like Netflix—even they were adamant about never getting into ads. If you go back and just listen to what Netflix was saying even 15 years ago, even Reed Hastings would have said, "You are out of your mind, we would never do that." Ultimately, they did it. To me, if you're going to do it ultimately, you can't really compete against companies that are using ads if you're not. It's very hard. If you're ultimately going to do it, you might as well start earlier because you have to build a culture around it and it just takes time. I don't think it's a big deal that OpenAI waited, but I was probably pushing for ads sooner than they've chosen to do it. I think now they probably are going to get it right. I'm so curious what you think is going to happen to the hyperscalers now. I saw this news report the other day that Anthropic's considering securing 10 gigawatts of their own power, which just makes me think they're going to have the power, so why don't they just create their own clouds effectively? The hardware might be different, more focused on inference etc. Does that jeopardize these business models, which I think people have thought of as pretty damn good at the hyperscalers? Do you think the future is different as a result of AI? I do. I mean, I've kind of thought this for probably about a year now, and I wouldn't say there's anything conclusive, but I am more confident in the thesis that the hyperscalers are a worse business model going forward. Now it's interesting because usually when you say something's a worse business model, you're implying that growth is going to slow or margins are going to contract. I actually think you're going to see the opposite. I think that AWS, Azure, and certainly GCP—I think these businesses are going to accelerate for a while, just because their customer bases, like Anthropic and OpenAI, are growing at an enormous pace. As they get to be a bigger part of the business, the growth will accelerate. The problem is that you went from a dynamic where AWS and Azure—and to some extent GCP—had a customer base of every corporation in the world. Therefore they had fragmentation and they had the benefits of massive economies to scale that no single company could get, and it was a very good business. The problem going forward is that I think it's highly unlikely that LLMs are not very concentrated in the hands of four or five companies. Those companies right now are investing a ton and they are cash flow negative. Therefore they're looking for compute anywhere they can get it. But if we're correct, at some point in the next 5 to 10 years, they will be generating enormous amounts of free cash flow. When that happens, I think that they are likely to insource the compute. Every year AI's going to be a bigger percentage of the workloads at any hyperscaler. So if you roll out 10 years from now, I think that the majority of the workloads will probably be AI. The LLMs will probably be providing a lot of those workloads, and I think that it will make economic sense to take it in-house. Right now I think they look at the hyperscalers as more of a financing mechanism. These are well-capitalized companies with big balance sheets, but I don't think these companies are better than them at building data centers. Building CPU clusters is different than building GPU clusters. Running inference on GPUs is very different than workloads on CPUs. I think the LLMs are actually better at inference than the hyperscalers. And then you have this whole dynamic of neoclouds. The initial view from most public investors was that this was like pure overflow capacity—that there weren't enough GPUs and these things would be dead as soon as Microsoft got its GPUs. I certainly would not make the case that they are fantastic businesses, but I don't think they're going away like people thought because I think they're better at running GPU clusters than the traditional hyperscalers are. And I think there's a lot of interest from Nvidia and other chip companies to make sure that the customer base is diversified. Nvidia has a very big balance sheet and they want to keep these players in business. So over the next 10 years, I think these hyperscalers will grow fast. I think the margins will be challenged both because the businesses are getting a lot more capital intensive and because the customer base is getting more concentrated. Meta is not a hyperscaler, but they insourced all their compute because they're just too big to use somebody on the outside. If you think about the last couple of years, probably the best thing you could have done is just be long the AI buildout in all its various forms, and maybe that will remain true moving forward. But it seems like the market is starting to think ahead to the other implications of AI. Software got absolutely decimated recently, and everyone thinks because of Claude Code and the amazing experiences people are having, software businesses are just screwed. I'm curious how you're starting to think now beyond just the AI build. It seems like AI is going to be here. Now the rest of the world has to start to absorb this technology. How are you starting to think beyond just the build? I'm super curious what you think about the software selloff, but even more broadly, like the real economy now has to start to swallow this new technology. It is incredibly difficult to know. And I don't think that's because I don't have perfect information; I think it's just that these models are improving at a rate which is exponential and understanding how that makes its way into the real economy and the implications is difficult. I think you probably want to use a few frameworks. It really comes down to which companies you think will have a moat in most circumstances. It's fairly straightforward to identify moats that are protected from the proliferation of digital intelligence. Once you get into robotics and other areas, you start to have to question the moats around some other traditional industrial companies. And globally, how do countries that were arbitrage in labor do relative to developed economies? I think there's going to be phases of this. The first phase is software. And that's really because Claude Code entered the zeitgeist and all of a sudden people see it and see on Twitter that people are saying, "Oh, I created a CRM system in like a day," and everyone thinks, "Oh my god, this is not good." That's kind of where people are now. We wrote in our letter at the end of the year that the buildout is still going to be a thing in terms of places to invest, but it's increasingly going to become about which companies are affected. There haven't been any shorts in AI. There basically were no shorts prior to 2024, really. If you wanted to just say, "I'm going to short something because of AI," you didn't make a lot of money doing that. In our letter I said there are going to be a lot of shorts and some longs because of AI. Software's the first one. The market tends to swing to extremes. My guess is that software will have to evolve and will probably be a worse business model going forward, but I think the same way that Walmart evolved with e-commerce, it will survive. Would they have preferred that e-commerce never happened? Probably, at least at the beginning. It required an enormous amount of investment, their margins took a hit, and they had new competitors. I think that'll be the case with software too, where companies that have really great distribution and great business models and are systems of record for companies will adapt. One of the things I did is I asked the LLMs, "Are you designing your own ERP system?" and they said, "No, we're buying a new ERP system from this company." Quite telling. If they're not doing it yet, at least you're protected for a few years. So I think systems of record are going to be difficult to displace. While it's neat to create software for small productivity enhancements, if you really want to run your entire business on something like an ERP or a CRM system, I think it's going to be quite a while before people are just "vibe coding" those systems. But I don't think that you can just sit back as a software company and say, "We're a system of record, we'll be fine." You're going to have to integrate AI and find ways to evolve the same way Walmart integrated e-commerce. It was painful for a long time, and hopefully on the other side of it... but this is fairly low conviction because everything about AI's impact on the economy is inherently low conviction. I think everyone is likely underestimating how much these models are going to improve. To really think about what's going to happen, you have to almost not think like an investor, you have to think like somebody who's into science fiction. Can you imagine a version of the story where this is all just overblown? Is there any coherent potential future where five years from now we're just like, "Actually these things weren't that big of a deal," or they were much less of a big deal than we thought they were going to be? The only way that would be the case—and even this I think wouldn't hold—would be if scaling laws just totally peter out. But even if scaling laws stopped and these models got no better, I think you probably have three years of just people learning how to incorporate AI into their daily lives or their companies. Certainly it wouldn't be good for the businesses if scaling laws stopped. I still think you'd have pretty profound changes within the economy. And betting that scaling laws are going to stop is a really low probability assumption. There's just nothing to suggest that's the case. In fact, everything suggests the opposite. And I think it's difficult to really get your arms around what that means. We went from "this is an interesting chatbot like Google" to "oh my god, these are going to be solving problems that humans can't do." We're already almost there. I have a 12-year-old son who's interested in investing. I think your son's interested in investing, we've talked about that before as well. What do you tell him about the future of this profession given these tools? Surely it applies to us too, and you know we may be smarter than it now but... Elon Musk says it's better to go through life being an optimist and be proved wrong than a pessimist and be proved right. To be young and interested in something and be dissuaded because AI's going to be better than you is a very self-defeating mindset. It is likely that at some point in the future everything that we do is arbitraged away by AI. I think it would be naive of me to say no. Do I think that's happening anytime in the next couple years? I don't. What do you tell someone to focus on? First of all, unless someone's really interested in something, they're not going to be good at it. So it might be the case that being a plumber or being an electrician is the most moated job in the world, but if you don't want to be a plumber it doesn't help very much. It's hard to tell your kids, "Don't do this or don't do that because it's going to be irrelevant." I saw a podcast recently with a Google researcher who laughed and said he doesn't even tell his daughter to study, just "go out and have a good time." I think that's a very destructive way of going through your life. You should go through life thinking that you want to achieve things, that you're interested in things, and you're curious, as if this technology doesn't exist. And if it turns out that whatever job you envision having no longer exists, then you'll have to adjust. Talk with John and Daniel about the GameStop story, we can touch on it here too. I'm curious what you most learned about yourself during that period of time when lore has it that in February of '21 you went to your team and said the way you'll calculate comp this year won't include January because it was just a completely insane period of time. You took certain steps to create stability in the business. In such a stressful period of returns, I'm just curious what you learned about yourself or what it was like emotionally to go through that time. It's incredibly difficult. I never want to come across as too exaggerated about my experience because there's people who go through a lot worse things in life. But as an investor, I'd say that was about as bad as it gets. We went from being top of the world where everyone thinks we walk on water to being in a position where everyone thinks we're going to go out of business. I have a lot of pride in what I do and I don't need to be celebrated, but I also really did not like having our firm and our performance dragged through the mud. Now granted, it deserved to be treated that way because the performance was very bad. It also is a bit lonely. During GameStop there were probably a couple other people going through the same thing you had. I found it helpful to go back and read and listen to Ken Griffin's interviews in 2008 and people that I respected. But it's lonely, it's a matter of testing your resilience. First of all, we never came close to going out of business; that was just nonsense. For me, I was never going to quit because even though we had made some mistakes, I deeply believed that we were still good at what we do and that we had something to offer the world. I was confident in that. But GameStop changed things—it was the beginning of a change in the market structure on the retail side. I knew we had to adapt to that, even if I didn't know exactly how that would play out. By that point, I'd been doing the job for 20 years. I never really had severe adversity, probably because at some point someone was very quick to risk manage. But I never really had that. And so I thought to myself, "Am I really going to be the guy who quits the first time there's a severe bump in the road?" The analogy that people gave was "one day at a time." Bill Ackman was like, "Look, every day try to do something that makes things a little bit better." When you have that kind of a drawdown, if I hit the ball out of the park for three months, investors would just say I'm volatile and crazy and they don't believe in me. If I slowly and methodically did it, some people would just give up because it was just too crazy. It's impossible to disprove the negative narrative in the short term. It takes years. Acknowledging that this was not going to be something that you change overnight was key. You weren't going to change people's perception of you as an investor or D1 as an attractive place to invest capital overnight no matter what you did. It was really like looking inwardly at the team, making sure that we were all on the same page and that no matter how many people outside might doubt us, we were going to do it. Assuming some did, what do you think of the people that redeemed from D1 during that time? I don't harbor any ill will. To some extent we deserved it. I appreciate it much more when people stayed. I always start out these dinners, even at the worst time, by saying, "Ask me anything. Criticize me. It is my job to deliver for you. If I don't do it, it's on me." Ultimately I think that when you screw up in business, capital follows returns. And when you deliver poor returns, capital will leave. We had a lot of great investors that stuck through it, and I deeply appreciate that more than I resent people redeeming. It's pretty asymmetric. LinkedIn Founder: AI Is Changing Every Job Faster Than You Think | Reid Hoffman

Silicon Valley Girl(1.5M) ·27:43 ·9.7K views · 2026-02-24
Reid Hoffman (Co-founder, LinkedIn) Marina Mogilko (Host, Silicon Valley Girl)
We are currently at only 5% of the AI revolution’s potential, transitioning toward a future where "individual contributors" are replaced by "conductors" who manage fleets of AI agents.
TL;DR We are currently at only 5% of the AI revolution’s potential, transitioning toward a future where "individual contributors" are replaced by "conductors" who manage fleets of AI agents. To avoid obsolescence, professionals must move beyond basic chatbots and develop a...

TL;DR

We are currently at only 5% of the AI revolution’s potential, transitioning toward a future where "individual contributors" are replaced by "conductors" who manage fleets of AI agents. To avoid obsolescence, professionals must move beyond basic chatbots and develop a "reflexive" habit of deploying AI for every task, from financial analysis to interpersonal conflict resolution.

The Evolution of the "Individual Contributor"

  • The Death of the Solo Worker: Hoffman posits that the era of the "individual contributing worker" is ending. In the near future, every professional will instead deploy and manage a set of AI agents to execute their work.
  • The "Conductor" Model: The role of the human is shifting from a practitioner (like a violin player) to a conductor.
      • Example: A software engineer will no longer just type code; they will manage 20 coding agents to generate, cross-check, and deploy software via voice commands.
  • AI Agents as Scalable Intelligence: AI should be viewed as intelligence that scales at the price of compute and electricity. It allows a single person to process massive amounts of information and execute complex workflows that previously required large teams.

Mastering the AI Maturity Curve

Hoffman categorizes AI usage into three distinct levels of proficiency:

  • Level 1: Basic (Voice-Led Prompting):
      • Voice-Billing: Instead of typing short prompts, speak to the AI. Human speech is faster and provides more context.
      • Prompt Engineering: Use the AI to write its own prompts. Speak your research goals to the AI and ask it to "write the perfect two-page prompt" to execute that research.
  • Level 2: Medium (Role-Based Multi-Agents):
      • Ascribing Roles: Assign specific personas to agents (e.g., "Act as a venture investor," "Act as a nuclear safety expert," "Act as a contrarian") to stress-test ideas.
      • Integrated Projects: Moving from one-off chats to persistent "projects" (like Claude Projects) that have access to performance data and specific brand goals.
  • Level 3: Advanced (Meta-Analysis & Data Integration):
      • The "Meta" Layer: Deploying agents to analyze other agents. For example, asking an AI to find "throughlines" across 30 different podcast transcripts to identify what is working and what isn't.
      • External Synthesis: Combining internal operational data with massive sweeps of external web research to identify trends before they hit the mainstream.

Market Disruption: The End of SaaS "Moats"

  • The $300 Billion Correction: Recent market volatility (prompted by Claude’s coding capabilities) signals a shift in how B2B software is valued.
  • Collapse of the "Billion-Dollar Barrier": Historically, SaaS companies like Salesforce had massive "moats" because a competitor would need to spend a billion dollars to replicate their feature set. AI lowers this barrier to entry, allowing new players to generate complex software (like a CRM) with minimal manual coding.
  • Human-Plus-AI Advantage: While AI can generate code, it cannot "walk around a business" to understand nuanced human needs. The most successful software will be a combination of AI efficiency and human-centered design.

Career Strategy: How to Double Income by 2026

  • Become an AI Transformation Agent: Businesses are desperate for "AI talent"—not just researchers, but people who know how to apply AI to supply chains, marketing, and risk analysis.
  • Public Demonstration: To increase earning potential, demonstrate your AI proficiency on platforms like LinkedIn. Companies are looking for "conductors" who can lead their legacy systems into the AI era.
  • Refactor Small Businesses: Small entrepreneurs must rebase their entire business model on AI.
      • Value Add: Since AI will make basic content and services free, entrepreneurs must pivot to "group experiences," personal branding, and high-trust relationships that AI cannot yet replicate.

The Future of Human Invention

  • 60-70% Collaboration: Hoffman estimates that the majority of future inventions will be "Human-plus-AI" created.
  • The 5% Rule: Only 5% of future "Eureka" moments will be truly unassisted human discoveries. The rest will be primarily AI-driven or AI-facilitated.
  • Reflexive AI Usage: The goal for 2025-2027 is to make AI usage a reflex. Before starting any task—from planning a vacation to having a difficult conversation with a family member—ask: "How can I deploy AI to help me do this better?"
"There aren't individual contributing workers anymore... we all deploy with a set of AIs... I’m more of a conductor than I am a violin player or a piano player." — Reid Hoffman
"Even most people who say 'Oh yeah, I'm using AI' are not using it seriously enough. In everything that I do, I think about: How would I use AI to help me do that?" — Reid Hoffman
"The lucrative jobs won't just be the super high-priced AI researchers... this year is when we really begin to see the applications. How do I run my business better? How do I do my supply chain or financial analysis better?" — Reid Hoffman

Inside Opendoor: The $2.8B Bet with CEO Kaz Nejatian

Sourcery with Molly O'Shea(25.7K) ·58:35 ·3.4K views · 2026-02-24
Kaz Nejatian (CEO, Opendoor) Molly O'Shea (Host, Sourcery)
CEO Kaz Nejatian is executing a radical, product-driven turnaround at Opendoor by stripping away corporate bureaucracy, replacing human-intensive processes with AI, and reintroducing "founder energy" to a public company that was previously in a "slow decline" under professional management.
TL;DR CEO Kaz Nejatian is executing a radical, product-driven turnaround at Opendoor by stripping away corporate bureaucracy, replacing human-intensive processes with AI, and reintroducing "founder energy" to a public company that was previously in a "slow decline" under...

TL;DR

CEO Kaz Nejatian is executing a radical, product-driven turnaround at Opendoor by stripping away corporate bureaucracy, replacing human-intensive processes with AI, and reintroducing "founder energy" to a public company that was previously in a "slow decline" under professional management.

Leadership Philosophy and Personal Stakes

  • Radical Financial Alignment: Nejatian receives a salary of only $1 and actually "pays to work" at Opendoor because he must cover his own benefits.
      • Incentives: His compensation is entirely tied to stock price levels the company hasn't seen in years, ensuring he is more leveraged on the company’s success than any other stakeholder.
  • The "Founder Chair": Nejatian argues that American corporations often decline when "professional managers" replace founder energy.
      • High Alpha vs. Beta: He views his role as sitting in the "founder chair" to call bullshit on mediocrity and pursue "extraordinary outcomes" rather than industry-standard "best practices."
  • Ignoring the Ticker: He maintains that the company drives the stock price, not vice versa. He does not have Yahoo Finance on his laptop and has removed stock tickers from the office to focus on "fighting for every inch."

Operational Efficiency and the AI Shift

  • Eliminating Bureaucracy: Upon joining, Nejatian reviewed every invoice and employee. He discovered a well-known consulting firm was being paid $50M to provide advice he summarized as: "do everything worse but cheaper."
  • Automating the Loop: Previously, a single home offer required 11 human touchpoints, including photographers and offshore workers manually circling repairs on Chromebooks.
      • Zero Human Intervention: In many current flows, AI handles the entire process, reducing the human count in the loop to zero.
  • Product-First Management: All product managers (ICs) report directly to the CEO. Nejatian personally reviews every project in the company weekly and tests customer service lines himself.
      • Support Quality: He recently fired an offshore support team in Mexico after a test call resulted in the agent telling him to "search Google" to figure out how to buy a home from the company.

Cultural Reconstruction

  • The End of Remote Work: On his first day, Nejatian mandated a return to the office with only seven days' notice. This led to "double-digit attrition," which he viewed as a necessary filter for commitment.
  • Hiring "The Open Army": Opendoor is aggressively hiring former YC founders to lead critical divisions, including the entire mortgage and escrow teams.
  • Radical Authenticity: Nejatian eschews traditional PR, noting that the company doesn't even have a PR department.
      • Direct Communication: He manages his own DMs on X (formerly Twitter) and views long-form, unscripted communication as the only way to remain authentic as a "non-well-rounded" leader.
      • Public Interest: A recent financial open house drew 55,000 live viewers, significantly higher than the average earnings call.

Board Dynamics and Strategic Mission

  • An Active Board: Unlike "shrimp cocktail" boards that meet quarterly, Nejatian texts his board members (including Keith Rabois and Eric Feder) almost daily.
  • Capital Allocation: The company has cleaned up a "ticking time bomb" on the balance sheet that existed when Nejatian took over; the balance sheet is now described as incredibly healthy.
  • The "Piece of America" Mission: The ultimate goal is to restore the ability for average workers (teachers, plumbers) to own homes, a prospect Nejatian believes is currently "deeply saddening" in its difficulty.
  • Next Steps: The company is currently beta-testing a new mortgage product and shipping updated underwriting and offer models.
"The company is not the stock price, the stock price is not the company. The company drives the stock price, not the other way around. So if you build a great company, the stock price will reflect that over time." — Kaz Nejatian
"We paid [consultants] $50 million for this. Their advice was basically the following tweet: do everything worse but cheaper. And so everything got worse, not cheaper, just worse." — Kaz Nejatian
"I find the best thing for culture is this: tell people who you are. Say things that people can reasonably disagree with, because if you say something everyone agrees with, you’ve said nothing useful." — Kaz Nejatian
"I think people misprice risk in their head and they think waiting reduces risk and in my experience it just fucking doesn't." — Kaz Nejatian
I paid a company to work here. I pay Opendoor money to work here. That's insane. My salary is one dollar and unless stock levels see levels they haven't seen in years, I don't get paid. If you look at the companies that have made a meaningful difference in the history of the world and have become good companies, basically all the founders or CEOs of those companies would have said words that sound like mine. Increase the stock price tomorrow by a penny or by a dollar; that's not the job. Over a long period of time, the stock price converges with the company. The company is not the stock price, and the stock price is not the company. The company drives the stock price, not the other way around. So if you build a great company, the stock price will reflect that over time. The only way to build a great company is to build something people want. The single largest expense on our income statement was millions of dollars paid to a well-known consulting firm. Their advice was basically the following tweet: do everything worse but cheaper. And so everything got worse—not cheaper, just worse. And our job is to make it easier for them to own a piece of America. Brex Presents. Molly O'Shea, Founder at Sourcery. Kaz Nejatian, CEO, Opendoor. Sourcery Presented by Brex. Kaz, welcome to Sourcery. Thanks for having me. I don't know if it feels like a long amount of time or a short amount of time for you, but I interviewed Keith when you first started and that was back in September of 2023. How have the last six months gone? I mean, it's been an interesting, I'd say deeply interesting experience. I have a couple of things that you kind of think about when you're prepping for a new job. I never thought I would leave my last job, so taking this job was not unlike a thing that I thought would happen. But in hindsight, it has been so deeply meaningful for me and my family. I judge my work week every week. I judge it based on the three vectors that I judge it, called hard, valuable, and fun. Was the week hard? Was it valuable? Was it fun? And it hasn't been that many weeks, but honestly, all of them have been hard, all of them have been valuable, and all of them have been fun. And it does genuinely feel like we're making progress every single day, which is very rewarding. And this was entirely disruptive to your life. You joined a company that was at 50 cents a share and it was about to be delisted. And then you get pulled in, recruited to this position. So what was it like going from Shopify where you—I don't know, maybe it was nice and easy and a little bit more linear—to something that is completely abstract? I remember when I joined Shopify in 2019, I left Facebook to join Shopify. And the number one question that my colleagues would ask me when I chose to join Shopify was, "What the fuck are you doing?" because back then, Shopify was very broadly shorted. The day I joined, there was a short seller report that came out saying Shopify would go bankrupt in 12 months. And honestly, even at the time, it was relatively obvious to me Toby had built this wonderful thing and people were misjudging it. But from the outside, Shopify back then was not the winner it is today. And the process—people forget, the process of Shopify over the last few years was not the easiest one. Going through COVID wasn't easy, coming out of COVID wasn't easy, building with AI wasn't easy, and getting out of the logistics wasn't easy. It wasn't easy, but it was rewarding, which is important. The reason I joined Opendoor was because from the outside, I had become obsessed with the company. I take apart companies for fun. That's what I do. I read about the history of companies and I take them apart. And in February 2023, I was flying from Dallas home. I remember this like it was yesterday. For reasons that I can't explain to you, I'm like, "Let's pick apart Opendoor, let's see what happened to it." And I was on a plane. From the time I took off to the time I landed, it was like a three-and-a-half or four-hour flight. I remember getting off the plane and being just outraged by what had happened to this company. I was just so angry about it. So then I spent almost every weekend thinking about Opendoor and going through Opendoor and using the product, figuring out what had happened. What made you angry? I think the problem Opendoor is trying to solve is a deeply meaningful one. And there's nothing worse to me than seeing opportunities wasted. When you see someone who doesn't know how to play basketball that well, if they play basketball poorly, they're like, "Well, you're not that good, go do something else." When you see someone who's an excellent basketball player just throw away the talent, everyone gets angry. Everyone's like, "No, this should turn out better." And Opendoor should turn out better. It got to the point where at dinner every night, my wife and I would talk literally only about Opendoor. To a point where my wife at one point was like, "Okay, we've talked about Opendoor enough, can we please just go buy some shares in the company so we can stop talking about this?" And I said, "Well, it's not really the type of company we can buy that much shares in because it's not that big." And then she said, "How big is it?" And I think the market cap was a few hundred million dollars back then. I'm like, "I don't know, a couple hundred million dollars." And then she said, "Well, why don't we buy it?" So my wife's plan literally, genuinely, honestly was like we would sell everything we owned and leverage ourselves as much as we could to take Opendoor private so we could fix it. So I called Keith back in think April and I was like, "Hey man, I'm thinking of doing this, will you do it with me?" So that was my intro to Opendoor, because from the outside it was just so obvious to me this problem was one worthy of solving. It was a problem that you should dedicate your life to solving because when kids grow up in homes that their parents own, their life outcomes are better. When people buy homes, crime goes down in the neighborhood. It's a deeply meaningful problem to solve and it's been basically untouched by technology. So I thought it was one worth solving, and a couple months into the job, I still feel as strongly about it as I did back then. How do you feel about doing this correction, this turnaround as a public entity versus a private entity? Did you ever think about taking it private? I was going to. That was my plan. Look, I think there's basically two reasons to take this company private and just only two. There's one way in which running a public company is harder than running a private company. One real way. And that is people will write things about you in newspapers. So if you care about what the Wall Street Journal writes about you, running a public company is difficult. Luckily for me, I just don't give a fuck. I do not care what things look like, I care a lot about what things are. And there's frequently a dissonance between those two things for a very long time. Over time, that dissonance gets smaller and smaller as we execute against the mission. But at the beginning, lots of people are like, "What the hell is this thing? It will never work," etc. And so that's if you care about that, this is harder. But I never got into fancy clubs when I was a kid. I never really cared about being in the Forbes 30 Under 30. I've never cared about these things. I care about other things, just not these things. So in that way, it's not harder for me. The second way in which you could say, "Well, we should take Opendoor private," and that is I think you could reasonably argue that it would be more monetarily good for me and my family because I believe in the company. We've bought shares on the open market. I just don't care about that. I just don't that much. My salary is a dollar for a reason. Yeah, let's talk about that. So when you joined, your salary is a dollar. You're weighted heavily on performance and that's what your equity package will potentially award you. How do you balance that and what is the motivation for you behind that versus having that job at Shopify? Look, I think there's two things that are true. Let me just say both of them. The first is this: I find that if you do something for money, the half-life of your ability to put up with pain is very low. Everyone intuitively understands this. But if you do something because you believe in the cause, you will be much more durable, you'll be able to do a lot more. So I do this; I come to work every morning. We work very hard at Opendoor because we believe in the mission. Everyone who works here believes in the mission, I believe in the mission, my family believes in the mission, my six-year-old son believes in the mission. We believe in this mission. So for us, we're on this journey because the outcome matters. It matters to us what happens to the average homeowner. And second, I do think—like I'm not indifferent to the stock price. There's no one who's more highly leveraged on Opendoor's stock price than I am. I'm number one by far. I paid a company to work here. I get paid a salary of a dollar but I have to pay for my benefits. So I pay the company, I pay Opendoor money to work here. And unless the stock price gets to levels that it has not been in a very, very long time, this would be a bad financial outcome for my family. I think that's insane. I don't know, I think generally like if you look at the companies that have made a meaningful difference in the history of the world and have become good companies, basically all the founders or CEOs of those companies would have said words that sound like mine. Imagine you're in a room with Jeff Bezos when Amazon dropped by 90%. Or you're in a room with Ernie from Carvana when Carvana dropped 90%. They didn't say their position would not have been "my job is to increase the stock price tomorrow by a penny or by a dollar." That's not the job. Over a long period of time, the stock price converges with the company. The company is not the stock price, and the stock price is not the company. The company drives the stock price, not the other way around. So if you build a great company, the stock price will reflect that over time. And the only way to build a great company is to build something people want. I don't actually understand this, like I don't have Yahoo Finance on my laptop, I just don't. You've walked around the office; the stock ticker is nowhere. I have a copy on my desk and there's a copy in the book bar of Bill Walsh's book 'The Score Takes Care of Itself'. Football players who look at the score all the time tend to be not very good football players. And people who watch ESPN and look at the football score tend to be not good football players. Our job is to focus on fighting for every single inch, every single yard every single day. And that tends to get you to the right place. We were talking about how you took over the company, you're coming in with a mission. But I think more importantly, you're really bringing a founder mentality into something that was dead. It was like a shell of a company and shell of a culture. And now there is literally a cult following. You have the army around you, the "Open Army." And then you have people like me and people like Keith who are just pounding the table and want more Opendoor. So could you share why it's so important to have a founder mentality with such a big turnaround like this? In order to create things that have not been created before, you need exothermic energy. We're not a widget factory. The thing we don't do is optimize the creation of a thing over and over again and try to turn like a dollar ten into a dollar twelve. That's not our job. If that was our job, then you can imagine like a professional manager being good at it—just looking at the knobs and turning them slightly. But I find in the history of American corporations—and for what it's worth, I think this is a uniquely North American problem because it's not true in Japan, for example—you get these significant moments of alpha followed by a slow decline once managers take over. And you can think of your favorite big company where it had that significant moment of alpha and then the decline happened. So I think the right words to use is not whether the founder is working at the company or not. The right words to use is whether the founder chair is taken or not. Is someone sitting in the founder chair or is there around every single meeting table in the company an empty chair from someone whose job it is to call bullshit on things? So I'm not anyone's ideal dream of a corporate CEO. If you go to Harvard Business School and imagine what a CEO looks like, it ain't me. But I'm very good at building products. And I care a lot about the details of everything. And what that allows me to do, and allows Opendoor to do, is to do things that are unusual. We are not going to be a beta company, we're a high alpha company. You can only generate that, you can only generate extraordinary outcomes, if you do extraordinary things. The best way to get the same outcome as everyone else is grab a consulting firm, ask them what is a best practice in X industry and do that. But what that gets you is what the industry does. Our job is to create a new world and you can't do that without founder energy. When you came in, I heard you read all the expense items line by line and you came across a very chunky consulting fee that probably did nothing. But could you talk about the process of coming in and what you cut immediately? I went through literally every single invoice the company had paid for the previous 12 months. I did two things: I went through every single employee and then every single expense. The capital allocation of a company is determined by who it hires and what we pay for. So I went through everything. And I was just genuinely shocked by how many consultants billed Opendoor money. It was just dozens and dozens of external consultants who billed Opendoor money for things that I thought were useless. There were consultants billing Opendoor money to create presentations for internal Opendoor meetings. No? Yeah, and I'm like this is insane. This is just the nuttiest thing. By the way, I think that's a common pattern in American corporations where there are external consultants whose job it is to help you create presentations so you can present it to other people inside your company. It's just insane. It's just nutty. So two interesting things happened. One is we were working on the earnings release that would announce me. It was a Google doc, we were working on the release and I'm reading this thing and I put in all caps at the top—this is before I was working here—I put in all caps above the doc "who is writing this, whoever it is can they please stop working here." And I didn't know transparently that the Google doc was open. I thought it was just me and the board member going back and forth. But turns out our external PR firm who was drafting the release was in the doc. So they resigned before I showed up. Which was good because we didn't have to pay them their 90 days notice period, which was helpful. Very helpful. Second thing happened was the single largest expense on our income statement was millions of dollars paid to a well-known consulting firm whose advice can be approximated as the following. We paid them $50 million for this. Their advice was basically the following tweet: do everything worse but cheaper. And so everything got worse, not cheaper, just worse. Because that's literally what happens when you come in and say, "Cool, you're gonna do everything you're doing right now but cheaper." And everyone rationally understands if you do everything you're doing right now at lower cost, you get worse quality. My five-year-old daughter could tell you this. But we spent millions of dollars to learn this lesson. So part of our job at Opendoor—and the company, the entire company, is now dedicated to this—if you go around, you'll see people have stickers, you can actually find them, that say "Charge us by the month, pay with your life." Visit turing.com/sourcery. When I talk to Keith, he mentioned a couple of very aggressive things that he wanted to happen to the company. He rejoined the board, you joined the company, and you're CEO. The board seems super hands-on. When I talked to him, he talked about how there were 1,400 employees. He wanted it to cut down to around 300. How has that happened? I think Keith's insight was a correct one, genuinely correct one, which is that for the output Opendoor was getting, it did seem to have an unusually high opex. My problem is also a different one, which is this: I am offended when people do things that human beings should no longer do. Like if you walked into an office building and you saw someone using the Gutenberg press to create an internal memo for distribution, you'd be offended. If you saw a bunch of people running around delivering mail like it's 1962, you'd be offended. It's like this is just not work human beings should do today because we've learned lots of stuff. So I think there was a lot of work happening at Opendoor where—let me give you an example. When I got here and you went to opendoor.com and typed in your address, we'd say, "Great, we're gonna send someone to your house to take pictures." Someone to come to your house to take pictures. Then we would ship these pictures to overseas where someone on a laptop, on a very bad laptop, on a Chromebook would look at them and circle the areas that needed to get repaired. Then it would send that to someone else overseas and then it would just do that down the chain. So between the time you put your address in and you got an offer, there'd be 11 human beings who touched that. That's insane. That's just an insane outcome. Today zero human beings are in that loop in many of our flows. AI does all of that, which means our human beings can do things that are actually good for the mission. Let me give you another example; this is actually my favorite one. Because of the advice we got from this consulting firm, we offshored customer support of Opendoor to Mexico. And in my second week here, I was sitting with the head of our customer support team, I'm like, "Let's call together our customer support line." Because I do this, I use our product. So I picked up the phone and called the customer support line and I said, "Hey, I'm Kaz, I would like to buy this house that I'm seeing on the website, how do I do that?" And the customer support agent said—I'm not kidding about this—said, "Have you heard of google.com?" No? I'm like, "Yes." He goes, "Could you type in google.com on your browser and type in how to buy a home from Opendoor?" And then it went down the chain from there. Like if you have human beings doing that work, it is bad for that person, bad for Opendoor and bad for capitalism. Like I think we should have—what we should be able to say is this: that every single person that works at Opendoor is the most efficient human being possible at the thing they do. I think that's the end goal of the company and we will work towards that every single day. So what employee changes have you made? I mean, look, the one of first things we did was Opendoor used to be a remote company. You've noticed we're no longer remote. On my first day at Opendoor I said, "Hey, we're going back to the office, everyone has seven days." And a bunch of our colleagues decided not to join us on that journey and left the company because they don't want to work for an in-person company. And we wish them the best. We had double digit attrition because of that and it was, you know, good people, we wish them the best but the company got smaller as a result of that. We have hired more YC founders into Opendoor since I started than any company. Why? Because I believe that you can only do extraordinary things with founders around or people who act like founders. So if you just walk the floor you'll see that there is a shocking number of people with an orange Y on their shirt. So quite a few of them are, you know, entire mortgage team is YC founders, our entire escrow team is YC founders. Like we have—and the company's goal is to become far more technical, to look more like a software company than a manufacturing company. And that's the path we're walking down. Has it been hard to maintain culture with that? If you're bringing in an entirely new culture to something that was—I don't think Opendoor had much of a culture. Transparently, I think what happens to companies when they're run by, like, professional managers is that culture atrophies. What happens—this is a real thing—people think people have a caricature in mind of what leadership is. They think of a job of a leader is to stand in front of a room and be like mascot as CEO. Like "ra-ra-ra, we shall go over that hill." That's not what the job is. The job is to hire great people, train them well, tell them what the priorities are and inspire them in that order. That's the order of importance. And I think lots of people, like, pretend culture is like beer on tap in the office. That's just lazy; that's the abdication of your responsibility to people's careers. So I don't think Opendoor had that much of a—I find the best thing for culture is this: tell people who you are. Say things that people can reasonably disagree with, because if you say thing everyone agrees with you've said nothing useful. Right? And then win together. The best thing for culture is doing hard things together in person. High school football teams have cultures because they do things together that are hard in person. What isn't that much of a culture is like the Dunder Mifflin office in 'The Office'; that's just not a culture. Having Taco Tuesday is not a culture, it's like a caricature of what other people should do. For you, it seems like you're entirely authentic to yourself. I listened to a couple of your interviews in the past. So how do you carry that in this role? It's generally hard and I talk to company transparently about this. I am not close to being well-rounded. I'm not a good approximation of an average human being. I'm just not—this is a very real thing—I'm edgy in basically every reasonable way one can be edgy. So I have two choices. Choice one is I can go become well-rounded and become a pale pink imitation of myself. Or two, I can be my like not become well-rounded, become edgier and surround myself by people who are edgy in the opposite way. I've chosen the second path because I don't want to pretend something I'm not. I don't ask anyone to pretend something they're not. And I think the best way to explain this is this: if you go to an Italian restaurant and complain that the sushi is bad, that's your fault. That's just your fault. But if you go to a place labeled restaurant where the menu includes literally everything on there that you can order and you order something that's bad, that's their fault. My job is to actually describe the type of place this is and lots of people will not want to work here. In fact, less than one in a hundred people will ever want to work here. That's fine. But we have a very high bar. Our career page tries to do a very good job repelling you and convincing you not to apply to come here. But once you're here, we want you to be committed to the mission. My job is to accurately describe what it feels like to work here and I think people say I'm honest about it. Before we started and the cameras were on, we were talking about how important attention to detail is and staying true to your quality bar. And so with that and this aggressive personality, how much of that and that personality and maintaining a quality bar do you think is synonymous with success? I don't know, honestly. I can tell you this, I try very hard to criticize things, not the people who created those things. I think it's kind of useless to hold your past self guilty. So if you see me in a product review I frequently say "this fucking sucks." I very rarely say "you fucking suck." The work is the work of Opendoor, so Opendoor has failed when it produces something that is not good. The individual people who worked on it may be excellent. We just have to keep working at that and that's like a real thing. I don't have another way of doing this. For what it's worth, I think there have been successful people who don't pay that much attention to details, who are relatively macro-driven. They place good macro bets on what the economy is going to do and don't pay that much attention to details and that can be a very successful or at least profitable career. I'm not that guy. I'm just not. I care a lot about the pixels, I care a lot about the API, I care a lot about the database. I care a lot about the words we say, I call our customer service lines, I talk to our sales people, I care that this thing that we put out there is a reasonable representation of ourselves and every day we should wake up and be deeply ashamed of it and want to make it better. So I don't know—I don't know the answer to your question is I don't know how much of that is important for the average person's success. I can tell you that I don't know how else to do it. Honestly, it's a very real thing. I grew up as an immigrant and I had a very hard time with cultural norms that were commonly understood if you grew up in North America. It is why America is the country it is because people have owned the ground they stood on and raised their family there and said, "I belong here, you cannot tell me what to do." The fact that's no longer true is deeply saddening to me. So I am massively driven by the mission. Like we talk about that teacher in Kansas City, that plumber in Arkansas, and our job is to make it easier for them to own a piece of America. And so that makes it incredibly rewarding. It doesn't mean it's not hard or lonely, but it is very rewarding. I love how much all that stuff enrages you, you're like "this is wrong, we need to redo it."
