The time between decision and action has been always short in my life. We've moved across country on one days notice. I took this job within—the entire negotiation for this job was like 10 days or something. So I've always been that person. But I also think that people misprice risk in their head and they think waiting reduces risk and in my experience it just fucking doesn't. Like it doesn't, right? All it does is take time away from time you could be spent doing other things. Like what's the worst that could happen, we'll just paint over that wall again. Let's just go do that thing and then move onto the next thing, you know? And that's core to your whole faster ethos. The faster is not a new line, I've been saying this my entire career. I value speed above everything else. My job is to learn more. All companies are literally all companies are a collective effort to learn and if we learn faster we do more. If we do things faster we will also be wrong more frequently. But we would have been wrong that frequently just over a more stretched out amount of time. So let's just learn faster. So who's on the board? I've looked at the list, it's an incredible list of names. I think there's a kind of two ways to build a board for a company. The first is you build a board such that people who are on the board get out of your way and show up to meeting once a quarter, have some shrimp cocktail, put up their hand and then get back on the private jet and go away. Like the most common way American boards are built: find people who sound impressive, who have had a career but who have usually been retired for many years, so therefore they don't have any recent experience. Put them on a board and just optimize for them saying yes over and over again. That feels like an obscene waste of time and money to me. Imagine having to do a song and dance in front of people where you know the answer will be yes. Like what is the point of that? Just deeply useless. The second thing you can do is this: say "Hey, who are the people where I would pay money to have dinner with them? Like who are the group of people that I would pay money to have dinner with such that I can talk about my job?" So I talked to one of our board members this morning. I talk—like I talked to one board member yesterday. So my frequency of communication with the board is way higher than the average CEO's. But we also understand that their job is to give advice and my job is to do things and I ask them for advice all the time. I think they thought I was kidding when I said they get text from me almost every day, but they do. And they're excellent—just genuinely I would put up our board against any other board. Actually I had a very good conversation with our board members before I joined and I talked to all of them before I joined. And I was lucky in that it was a double opt-in system for me and the board. Like "Cool, we're like in this together." I remember having conversations with one of them like 3 am one night where I'm like, "Hey, I will do this but you have to commit to also do this for next three years. You and I are doing this together." And I had similar conversation with a few of them. And my ask of you is always ask is this actually weird or does it look weird? If it looks weird, please give me rope. If it is weird, push back, ask me questions. So that's been like primary back and forth. And so what we have is a more involved conversation where we don't go from three months without talking. I think for my first month at job I talked to one board member at least every day. Because like we're—when I got here the company's balance sheet had like a ticking time bomb on it. Like it wasn't obvious how the company was going to survive that ticking time bomb. Now luckily we've cleaned it up, the balance sheet is incredibly healthy but for the first like 10 days it was just not that obvious. What's the weirdest thing you've done? For what it's worth I don't recognize it when I do weird things. Like people afterwards tell me that was weird. But they don't—they don't feel weird to me, otherwise I probably wouldn't do them. Stablecoins Will Send Bitcoin MUCH HIGHER

Anthony Pompliano(644.0K) ·19:02 ·29.0K views · 2026-02-24
Bo Hines (CEO, Tether US) Anthony Pompliano (Host, The Pomp Podcast)
Former White House crypto advisor and current Tether US CEO Bo Hines details the launch of USAT, a Genius-compliant stablecoin designed to bridge global emerging markets with U.S.
TL;DR Former White House crypto advisor and current Tether US CEO Bo Hines details the launch of USAT, a Genius-compliant stablecoin designed to bridge global emerging markets with U.S. capital markets while acting as a massive institutional on-ramp for Bitcoin. The "Genius"...

TL;DR

Former White House crypto advisor and current Tether US CEO Bo Hines details the launch of USAT, a Genius-compliant stablecoin designed to bridge global emerging markets with U.S. capital markets while acting as a massive institutional on-ramp for Bitcoin.

Regulatory Landscape and White House Legacy

  • The "Genius" vs. "Clarity" Debate: Hines highlights the transition from his White House role to the private sector, focusing on the legislative momentum of the Genius Act (passed July) and the pending Clarity Act.
      • Clarity Act Outlook: Hines estimates an 80-90% chance of passing, noting that the remaining hurdles are less about yield and more about UX Structure—specifically whether issuers must be OCC-chartered banks.
  • Strategic Bitcoin Reserve (SBR): Confirmed that the SBR was established via executive order, though the goal remains to cement it through permanent congressional action.
  • President’s Working Group Report: Hines points to this report (available at crypto.gov) as the most comprehensive regulatory framework ever produced for the digital asset ecosystem.

Tether’s Market Dominance and Scale

  • Growth Metrics: Tether currently serves 530 million customers globally, growing at a rate of roughly 30 million per quarter.
  • Profitability and Lean Operations: Despite having only 300 employees, the company is projected to see $10 billion in profit in 2025.
  • Global Asset Reserves:
      • Gold: Tether is currently the 13th largest holder of gold in the world.
      • Treasuries: Ranked as the 17th largest holder of U.S. T-bills globally (including sovereign states), with the goal of becoming a top 10 purchaser this year.
  • Technology Investment: Beyond stablecoins, Tether is a prolific investor in robotics, infrastructure, and brain-computer interfaces.

Launching USAT and Financial Corridors

  • Institutional Integration: Tether US (USAT) was created as a Genius-compliant product specifically to service U.S. institutions through Anchorage Digital Bank (an OCC-chartered issuer).
  • Creating 24/7 Market Corridors:
      • Settlement Efficiency: USAT aims to move markets from T+2 or T+1 settlement cycles to T-0 (instant settlement).
      • Global Access: This infrastructure allows foreign entities (e.g., Korean banks) to participate in U.S. capital markets even on Friday afternoons when traditional rails are closed.
  • Consumer Use Cases: Hines envisions a future where payroll companies pay employees daily in stablecoins, allowing for automated, programmatic remittances to emerging markets like LatAm or Asia.

The Relationship Between Stablecoins and Bitcoin

  • The Ultimate On-Ramp: Hines argues that stablecoins are the first introduction to the digital ecosystem; once users are comfortable with on-chain movement, they naturally gravitate toward Bitcoin as the "granddaddy" investment vehicle.
  • Bitcoin Maximalism: Tether reserves include significant Bitcoin holdings, and the company is heavily involved in Bitcoin mining and trading.
  • Institutional Adoption: Noted recent moves by Bank of America allowing wealth managers to allocate to Bitcoin as evidence that the "infancy" stage of adoption is ending.

Future Outlook and Strategic Risks

  • Market Consolidation: Hines predicts "immense" consolidation of both blockchains and stablecoins over the next 5–10 years.
  • The Wallet Race: Tether is developing a Wallet Development Kit (WDK) to control the "point of origin" for the customer, believing that whoever owns the wallet interface will win the long-term consumer battle.
  • Privacy Concerns: Identified privacy as the most critical challenge to address over the next few years as consumer demand for private yet compliant transactions grows.

***

Notable Quotes

"Tether is arguably the most important player in the crypto ecosystem that’s not a commodity... We’re the 13th largest holder of gold in the world and we’re also huge Bitcoin maxis." — Bo Hines
"Stablecoin adoption from the institutional side brings obvious savings to banks and other merchants alike. You go from T-2 to T-1 to T-0 with stablecoins. You’re not waiting for intermediary settlements any longer." — Bo Hines
"The difference between Tether (USDT) and USAT is that USDT is the international issuer; USAT is issued by Anchorage Digital Bank, which is a fully licensed OCC chartered bank here in the US." — Bo Hines
"Stablecoins will be the first introduction to the digital asset ecosystem that people have... it’ll be an invitation for people to actually use [Bitcoin] as an investment vehicle of choice." — Bo Hines
